Morgan Stanley TMT Conference 2026 | Jensen Huang on AI, Compute, Tokens and the New Global Economy

Morgan Stanley(84.3K) ·42:53 ·21.8K views · 2026-03-18
Jensen Huang (CEO, NVIDIA)
NVIDIA CEO Jensen Huang asserts that the global economy has shifted to a "token-based" model where compute capacity is directly correlated to corporate revenue and national GDP.
TL;DR NVIDIA CEO Jensen Huang asserts that the global economy has shifted to a "token-based" model where compute capacity is directly correlated to corporate revenue and national GDP. He characterizes NVIDIA's data centers as "AI Factories" and predicts a near-future where the...

TL;DR

NVIDIA CEO Jensen Huang asserts that the global economy has shifted to a "token-based" model where compute capacity is directly correlated to corporate revenue and national GDP. He characterizes NVIDIA's data centers as "AI Factories" and predicts a near-future where the entire software industry evolves from tool-renting to renting "digital experts" (AI agents).

The Evolution of the Token Economy

  • Three Inflection Points: Huang outlined the rapid shift in AI capabilities over the last two years:
      • Generative: The initial "ChatGPT moment" where information was converted into tokens.
      • Reasoning: The introduction of "o1" models that use self-reflection to ground answers in truth, requiring 1,000x more compute than the first generation.
      • Agentic: The current shift toward "OpenCLA," which has surpassed Linux in adoption speed. These agents use tools and perform complex tasks autonomously, consuming millions of times more tokens.
  • From Queries to Actions: The fundamental nature of user prompts is changing from informational queries ("What is?") to task-based intentions ("Create," "Build," or "Do").
  • Software Transformation: Huang predicts that no "dumb" software will exist in the future; every application will be agentic. Software companies like Cadence and Synopsys will move from licensing tools to selling specialized tokens produced by digital experts.

The Economics of the "AI Factory"

  • Compute as Revenue: Data centers are now "AI Factories" producing a monetizable product: tokens. Huang argues that companies and countries are now "factory limited," meaning their top-line growth is restricted only by their available compute.
  • Tokens Per Watt: In a power-constrained world, efficiency is the primary competitive moat.
      • The CEO Review: Because power is scarce, choosing the right architecture is now a CEO-level decision. If a facility has a fixed gigawatt capacity, the architecture with 10x better "tokens per watt" generates 10x more revenue.
      • Inference King: Huang cited external analysis naming NVIDIA the "inference king" due to its order-of-magnitude lead in tokens per dollar.
  • The New IT Industry: The $2 trillion IT industry is pivoting from tool-renting to token-consumption, which will fundamentally expand the total addressable market (TAM) for software.

NVIDIA’s Full-Stack Strategic Advantage

  • Annual Innovation Cycles: NVIDIA's ability to release new infrastructure every year stems from owning the entire stack: CPUs, GPUs, NVLink, and AI Ethernet (Spectrum-X).
      • This vertical integration prevents the "cats and dogs" problem of trying to synchronize disparate third-party components.
  • Strategic Balance Sheet: NVIDIA uses its massive cash flow to secure supply chain capacity (wafers, memory, packaging) years in advance.
      • Security for Customers: Huang noted that when major partners like Microsoft (Satya Nadella) need gigawatts of capacity, NVIDIA’s ability to secure the entire supply chain—from copper to multilayer capacitors—is a strategic asset.
  • Forward-Deployed Engineering: NVIDIA has utilized "devtech" engineers for 33 years to integrate its libraries directly into the world’s software (e.g., Epic’s Unreal Engine), creating a proprietary ecosystem that is difficult to replicate.

Future Frontiers: Physical AI and OpenAI Updates

  • OpenAI Investment: Huang confirmed a $30 billion investment in OpenAI, noting that the lab is expected to go public toward the end of the year. He also highlighted that NVIDIA is aggressively ramping capacity for OpenAI across Azure, OCI, and AWS.
  • Physical AI: The next "trillion-dollar" frontier involves AI with physical awareness and causality (understanding gravity, collision, and object permanence).
      • Frontier Models: NVIDIA is leading this shift with models like Cosmos (physical AI), GR00T (humanoid robotics), and Alpomo (autonomous vehicles).
      • Digital Biology: The company is co-innovating with firms like Eli Lilly to build AI factories specifically for drug discovery and biological modeling.
  • Market Outlook: Huang predicts that within two years, agentic AI will be "done" (fully adopted), and the conversation will shift entirely to the decade-long era of Physical AI.

Oaktree's Howard Marks on Unpredictablility, Importance and Investing in AI

Bloomberg Television(3.1M) ·29:53 ·23.1K views · 2026-03-18
Howard Marks (Co-Chairman, Oaktree Capital Management)
Artificial intelligence has rendered the global investment landscape more unpredictable than at any time in history, while a 17-year period of easy credit has created a "frothy" market where high standards have been replaced by FOMO (fear of missing out) and lower safety margins.
TL;DR Artificial intelligence has rendered the global investment landscape more unpredictable than at any time in history, while a 17-year period of easy credit has created a "frothy" market where high standards have been replaced by FOMO (fear of missing out) and lower safety...

TL;DR

Artificial intelligence has rendered the global investment landscape more unpredictable than at any time in history, while a 17-year period of easy credit has created a "frothy" market where high standards have been replaced by FOMO (fear of missing out) and lower safety margins.

The AI Impact: Unpredictability and Investment Strategy

  • A "Less Predictable" World: Marks argues that AI makes the future harder to forecast than ever before. He emphasizes that investing requires two things: a view of what will happen and a view of the *probability* that the view is correct. AI significantly lowers the probability of being right.
  • Technology vs. Investment: While AI as a technology may not be a bubble (and its impact may even be underestimated), the *investments* in AI companies are subject to different rules.
      • Equity vs. Debt: Marks advises against lending to AI companies with fixed returns. If an investor is taking "fundamental business model risk" in a volatile sector, they should seek the upside of equity ownership rather than the limited return of a loan.
  • Labor Displacement: Marks highlights the efficiency and ruthlessness of AI, noting that the company Block recently cut 40% of its workforce because AI could perform tasks "cheaper and faster."

The Current State of Private Credit

  • Loss of "Specialness": In 2011, private credit offered high rates and safety as banks retreated. Today, the influx of capital has driven interest rates and safety covenants down.
      • Equilibrium: Marks notes that with public credit yielding ~7% and direct lending at ~8.25%, the 125-basis-point liquidity premium is "fair but not lush." The "specialness" of the asset class has largely vanished as it reached equilibrium with public markets.
  • Retail Risks: Marks expresses concern regarding the expansion of private credit vehicles (like BDCs) to retail investors who may not fully understand the ramifications of illiquidity or the absence of mark-to-market pricing.
  • Cycle Maturity: He compares the current enthusiasm for private credit to the movie *Casablanca*, suggesting that investors are only "shocked" by risks—like illiquidity—when the market turns, even though those risks were always present.

Market Cycles and the "Default Vacuum"

  • The 17-Year Exception: Since 2009, the market has seen an unusually long period of low defaults, aided by aggressive Federal Reserve intervention. This has conditioned investors to disregard the possibility of failure.
  • The "Naked Swimmer" Phase: Marks cites Warren Buffett, noting that "it's only when the tide goes out that we find out who's swimming naked." He believes the market has not yet seen the "washout" required to reveal who lent money unwisely during the good times.
  • Early Warning Signs: Marks points to recent bankruptcies and potential frauds (referencing "Cockroaches in the Coal Mine") as indicators that the "music" may be stopping. He notes that the worst loans are invariably made during the best of times.

Human Intuition vs. Machine Logic

  • AI as a Predictive Tool: AI does not "answer questions" so much as it "makes predictions" based on historical patterns and data. While it can process information without emotional or arithmetic error, it lacks essential human qualities.
  • What AI Lacks:
      • Intuition: AI cannot experience the "hairs standing up on the back of your neck" when reading a suspicious prospectus.
      • Character Judgment: Marks believes humans remain better judges of "bad people" and management integrity than algorithms.
  • Oaktree’s Stance: Oaktree uses AI (specifically tools like Claude) to marshal data and identify patterns, but the firm refuses to invest based on AI hypotheses alone. Marks maintains a "cautious" stance, waiting for a significant market decline before becoming aggressive.
"Life under AI is one of the most unpredictable things I've ever contemplated... the same power that gives it its importance gives it its unpredictability." — Howard Marks
"What the wise man does in the beginning, the fool does in the end... the early success becomes visible, other people emulate, the entrants bid for the thing in question, and the specialness goes away." — Howard Marks
"I think AI understands history, recognizes patterns, makes extrapolations... but I don't think it has intuition. I don't think AI has hairs on its neck." — Howard Marks
"The worst of loans are made in the best of times." — Howard Marks
I am always honored to get to speak with Howard, who is a deep thinker at a time where we need some deep thoughts. I want to start with your latest memo. You were talking about artificial intelligence and how it is definitely not a bubble as a technology. If anything, we might be underestimating its expectations. We'll get to that in a second. It's not the same thing to say about the actual investments in AI, that they're not in a bubble. We're all here because a lot of people are very concerned about private credit right now. Do you think that the concerns are warranted? About private credit? Yes. Well, I mean, you know, lending money to companies is a fundamentally sound activity. I've been engaged in lending money to sub-investment grade companies now for 48 years since I fortunately was asked by Citibank to start the high yield bond activity in '78, which was the beginning of that world. And, you know, our clients have done very well. I think 99% of the high yield bonds we bought paid off. So, you know, there's nothing wrong with it fundamentally, intrinsically. The problems arise when from time to time too many people want to do it. They're too eager to do it, they compete for deals, that bids down interest rates, that bids down safety, and then once in a while everything in the investment business gets sold to the wrong people who aren't prepared and are shocked when the warts show up. Do you think that the introduction of artificial intelligence and the displacement of certain corporate activities—the sort of disintermediation, the idea that software might be rendered obsolete—do you think that that changes the story of how you invest in any way? Well, I think that the change that is underway today, and in particular the introduction of AI, render the world much less predictable than at any time, probably any time ever, and certainly any time in my lifetime. Most people in the investment business decide their course of action based on what they think is going to happen. I argue that that's not enough. You need two things. I wrote a book about cycles in '18 and I said in there you need two things. One is a view of what's going to happen, and the other is a view of the probability that you're right. Not all predictions have the same probability of working out. And I think that life under AI is one of the most unpredictable things I've ever contemplated. And we just don't know. By the way, the same power that gives it its importance gives it its unpredictability. You know, what will it do, what won't it do, to what extent will it take over from us, to what extent will it put people out of work, what will a society be like if large numbers of people are put out of work? We've never contemplated questions like these. Which is the reason why I think people are concerned about the concentration of certain types of investments by private fund managers; that the lack of transparency is making people uneasy. A sense of they're not sure how those loans were made. Is there anything in the way in terms of the leverage or the covenants that were used that makes you think maybe we're getting to the end of a cycle, or that feels a bit frothy and that people are right to say maybe there needs to be a washout? I've spent the last three days writing a memo, that may not surprise you, about private credit. These days I speak a lot at occasions like these and to our clients and CFA groups and so forth, and everybody has questions, but at any given point in time the questions are usually the same for obvious reasons. In the last few months, or certainly the last six weeks, the main question has been "What about private credit?" Well, a year ago I wrote a memo called "Give Me Credit" and I said in there the only question I was getting was what about private credit, but in a good sense. What about private credit? Do we have enough? Should we put in more? You know, is it the key to riches without risk? And today they're asking what about private credit but in a different vein. And you know, I've been around long enough to have seen many cycles of boom-bust—not economic boom-bust, but boom-bust of an idea. You know, something new comes along. It's invariably something new because the new thing fires the imagination and is easily sold to people. Because it's new, there's never been a chance to see its flaws in action. So these things, I mean I don't think there's ever been a bubble in something prosaic. There was never a steel bubble, you know, or a hamburger bubble. But it's new technologies or new financial innovations. And so people buy in on the basis of promise without an understanding of the downside and then something happens which causes their expectations to be disappointed. Then they find out that they didn't know 100% of what they were doing, that they hadn't understood the limitations and you know, they end up unhappy. When you were writing "Give Me Credit", there was a discussion about how maybe at that time public markets might be more valuable or more attractive because of the enthusiasm around private credit. Are we at a place where there is enough fear to find this moment attractive or does there need to be even more fear? Are people still greedy? Is there still sort of enough heat in that system to be worked through? Yeah, I don't know how private credit is being priced today. Maybe Bob can tell us. In 2011 when the banks, chastened and regulated because of the global financial crisis, pulled back from lending for buyouts, so-called non-bank lenders stepped in and started to engage in direct lending, lending for midsize buyouts. And of course since the demand for capital from the private equity industry was strong and the supply of capital from the non-bank lenders was limited, the non-bank lenders could demand high interest rates and good safety. But you know, the greatest saying in our business is that what the wise man does in the beginning, the fool does in the end. What happens is the early success becomes visible, other people emulate, the entrants bid for the thing in question, and the specialness goes away. So I think enough of that took place in private credit for the interest rates to be bid down and the safety to be bid down. Let's say three months ago, two months ago, I would say that public credit was yielding about seven and direct lending was yielding about eight and a quarter. It struck me that 125 basis points for a liquidity premium was about fair. It was probably adequate but certainly not lush. And so in my opinion, the specialness had gone away. Private was at equilibrium with public. It was a fair deal but not more. And you know, I said in the memo everybody asked me what about private credit and I answer what about credit. Now of course there's an activity in our world that's called talking your own book and Oaktree has been in the public credit business for 48 years so we want some attention paid to that. But I think that when A and B are priced at equilibrium with each other, rather than putting all your money into one or the other, you might as well buy both. One of the other problems that people have identified in the private credit space is that increasingly there is an effort to reach out to retail investors. There is an effort to bring in people who might want periodic liquidity on some level. Do you think that the transparency of some of the assets or the marks—the pricing, the values—needs to be more standardized in order to really make this worthwhile? Well, obviously my guess would be that, well first of all, certain parties—of which we are not exempt—decided that they could do some business by selling private credit vehicles like BDCs to retail investors or retirement investors. And they did so. And I'm going to venture a guess that not all of the buyers were fully informed or took to heart the ramifications of illiquidity and the absence of a market to mark to. But they invested anyway, probably out of ignorance rather than knowing all about it and saying it wasn't a concern. And you know, I was thinking—I was speaking about this yesterday to another group of investors and I said it reminded me of "Casablanca". Has everybody seen the movie "Casablanca"? The gendarme goes into the bar that he's been taking bribes from for the last 20 years and he says, "I'm shocked, shocked to see that gambling is taking place." And you know, the point is the things that investors have discovered and are unhappy about are nothing new. These vehicles were always illiquid. They were never priced mark-to-market, etc. But you know, things are overlooked in happy times when people are eager to invest that then are the source of consternation when the stuff hits the fan. People have identified private credit in part because what you can't see makes people nervous. There are though these sort of questions about the software industry or other industries that could be affected by artificial intelligence. Do you think that some of the stress that you're seeing in some of these private credit funds or even the discussion around it is representative of a larger stress that will percolate out in a bigger default cycle or something more significant throughout the credit market? Well look, Lisa, there are credit cycles. There are times when everything's going well, it's really easy to borrow money. When things are going poorly, it's hard to borrow money. When it's easy to borrow money, people compete for the deals and as I described before, the interest rates tend to contract. In the tough times, people aren't that eager to lend so the few who will lend can demand higher interest rates. And this is the cycle that goes on. And you know, back in the fall when First Brands and Tricolor shocked everybody with their bankruptcy and their probable fraud, I wrote—and Jamie Dimon said what he said about there usually not being just one cockroach—I wrote a memo entitled "Cockroaches in the Coal Mine", mixing metaphors. And you know, I said when the music's playing, the money's available freely and that causes the lenders to compete for deals by lowering their standard. It creates a climate in which an undeserving credit can get money and a fraud has a higher probability of being perpetuated. It's life. One of the long-standing sayings in the banking business is that the worst of loans are made in the best of times. And it's for this reason. And so we went through 17 years of good times. You know, the stock market bottomed March 6th, I think it was, of 2009, 17 years ago this month. And there hasn't really been a tough time in the financial markets since then. Okay, 2015 wasn't great, we had three bad weeks in March of 2020 with the pandemic and then '22 was a poor year. But you know, since roughly September 30th of '22, I would venture that the S&P has doubled. I mean company values haven't doubled—intrinsic values—but prices have doubled. So it's been a great time and great times encourage the desire to put money to work and discourage analysis, discipline, high standards, and skepticism. FOMO takes their place. And when FOMO is ruling rather than skepticism, bad deals get done. And so this is nothing new. We've had 17 years of low defaults. The actions of the Fed made the global financial crisis, which was probably the most destructive environment I've ever lived through, have only one year of elevated defaults on high yield bonds rather than the normal two. The defaults in that instance—it was the worst incident and it had fewer defaults than any of the crises I had lived through. And then of course we had people talking about 15% default rates in 2020 and I think it came in at 5.5. So people just have gotten used to the absence of the defaults. Defaults are a normal thing and especially after you go through a period in which credit is readily extended, then you go into a period where the warts are disclosed. Buffett says everything the best, and he says it's only when the tide goes out that we find out who's swimming naked. It's only in tough economic times that we find out who lent money stupidly and to whom. And that period lies ahead. Is the corporate debt market more broadly pricing in an elevated default cycle in any way, shape, or form? Well, as I said, I don't know—but we have a bench here of Weinberg Parella guys, they could tell me what the rates are today. But you know, you would have to say not. I mean, what people mostly have been asking about is why are yield spreads on sub-investment grade credit at the low end of the normal range. So if the yield spreads are at the low end of the normal range, you would have to say that the fear of elevated defaults is not present and compensation for an elevated default rate is not available. We're talking about how AI and technology can transform some of our expectations just simply because it's so unpredictable and as you were saying, it's the most unpredictable environment that you've been in. Does it make sense to you that Google, Microsoft, Amazon—behemoths, very strong companies—are selling debt for 30, 40, 100 years at a time when we can't game out the next five? Well, I think that I think that that is exemplary of what I'm talking about here. I don't think if you think about what Lisa just described—and I think Google issued 100-year bonds paying 5.8%—I think you would have to say optimism, not pessimism, credulousness, not skepticism are in the ascendancy today. And when optimism and credulousness are in the ascendancy, it gets hard to make investments that will produce what we call excess returns, that is to say returns which are more than commensurate with the risk. How do you invest in a company that is tied to some of this new technology given that it does have such promise with any certainty? How do you select the companies that are going to benefit? Well, you know, first of all many more companies will be affected than just the AI companies or just the technology companies. But you know what I said in the December 9th memo, I touched on the wisdom of lending money for AI companies or tech companies to invest in AI. And basically, I quoted my colleague Bob O'Leary as saying that you probably shouldn't. You know, if you're going to put money into a company exposed to AI, you should probably buy the stock and get the upside if it ensues rather than just lend the money and get a fixed return if they're successful. And I think that's right. You know, I mean your expression was you're lending money to companies for 30, 40, 100 years when we can't predict the next five. So if you're taking fundamental business model risk, shouldn't you get paid for it by being an owner rather than a fixed income investor? Of course AI is going to affect every firm. Well, it is. It is and it is the fact that it's going to have such a sweeping effect and its unpredictability that renders this a tougher world. I like the old world better. I don't know about you. You know, when I was a kid... You walked to school backwards with barefoot. Well, nothing changed. You know, nothing changed from year to year when I was a kid and now things change every day. And that imparts a certain dynamism and certainly we live better than the world lived when I was a kid in many ways. But it does mean that we can depend less on our expectations. How is AI transforming what you do at Oaktree? Is it affecting, you know, the number of people you hire or the number of people you employ? Is it something that everyone's using as sort of a tool? It has not affected our business model, the number of people we hire or how we do our work. But it's just an aid. We're using it as an aid to marshal data. My experience with writing that memo—so I wrote the memo as Bob described and I do these things with the help of my son who is a great venture capitalist. And so when I got through writing it, I said, "Do you want to see it?" He says, "No, send it to Claude." So to send it—I wrote it on my desktop and to send it to Claude I had to copy it, paste it to an email, send the email to myself so that I could open it on my phone, copy it there and paste it into AI and send it off to Claude. So that took me a couple minutes to do that. And I pushed send and scrolled down to the bottom and the answer was there already. It was remarkable. I mean I was just blown away by this process. So you know, I mean AI can do these things. It has read everything that's ever been written. It remembers everything it's read and it can find it again, which is different from us, right? And it can find the patterns that have led to success or failure in the past and it can extrapolate those into the future. It can probably do so without making arithmetic mistakes or logical mistakes and it can do so without making emotional mistakes. It doesn't get too excited at the top or too depressed at the bottom. So these are—this is a big improvement over what 80% of people can do, 90%, I don't know. I'm hoping it's not an improvement over what 100% of people can do. I hope there are still things we can do, and I think there still are. Because, and I in the February memo there's a section called "Implications for Investing," and I said that I think there are things that AI can't do. I don't think it has intuition. You know, you read—sometimes you read a prospectus and as we say the hairs on the back of your neck stand up. I don't think AI has hairs on its neck. We believe that we've saved our clients a lot of money by not investing with bad people. I think we're probably a better judge of bad people than AI is. And and on and on like that. So I think that AI understands history, recognizes patterns, makes extrapolations. What AI basically does I've come to realize is it makes predictions. It doesn't answer questions, it makes predictions. And if you want a great example, you take out your phone, you compose an email and if you look at the bottom, it proposes the next word. It predicts what the next word in that sentence should be. "I hope you'll go with me to the..." party. It says it. Now it could be ballgame and maybe that's somewhere else down there, but it predicts the next word. And it does so because it's read 10 million sentences that start with "I hope you will go with me to the" and 73.7% of the time the next word was party. So it can do that. And it gives us predictions which I describe as hypotheses. But I'm not going to invest money for people on the basis of what it says. I think we still need humans to examine the hypotheses. But of course that reintroduces human error. But I still think it's an essential thing and I think we're not ready to turn the process over to AI, but we're glad to have its help. Do you think that in a time of such unpredictability it's important to have a greater degree of liquid assets or assets that can easily turn into capital that you can redeploy? Well, you know, when I started at Citibank in September '69, the bank and most of the money center banks—it's an old-fashioned expression—invested in what were called the Nifty Fifty. These were the 50 bestest and fastest-growing companies in America, where nothing could go wrong and there was no price too high. So if you bought the stocks the day I got there, I think it was September 22nd of '69 if I'm not mistaken, and if you held them tenaciously for five years, the greatest companies in America, you lost about 95% of your money. And then I was exiled to the Siberia of the bond department and asked to start a high yield bond fund, which was the luckiest thing in my life, or one of. And now I'm making money steadily and safely in the worst public companies in America. So what it taught me is that there is no asset that is so good that it can't become overpriced and lethal, and there are very few things that are so bad that they can't get cheap enough to be attractive. Despite our worries about AI and despite the inability to have confidence in our judgments, I still believe that assets, certain assets, have the potential to get cheap enough that they're a buy in that environment. Now exactly how we feel when we come to that moment remains to be seen; it hasn't happened yet. Very few people are absolutely throwing things away as we have seen in past crashes. But I think those things will happen and I like to think that we'll step up. We stepped up massively in September and October of '08 even though most people thought that the financial—or acted as if the financial sector was going to melt down. Do you think that we're headed toward another event like that? Who knows? I mean you know Ben Graham said that in the long run the market is a weighing machine but in the short run it's a voting machine. I don't know how people are going to vote next month. If they vote for crash, we'll have a crash. If they regain their equanimity and they decide we're okay, then we won't have a crash. It's as simple as that. And so you know, I'm not going to invest now in the belief that the crash isn't coming so I better put my money in now. I'm going to wait and see what happens. If it comes, I I think we'll become aggressive. But exactly when it's going to come I don't know and when it has arrived you don't know. You never know when it's cheap enough, but you have a sense. By the way, I'm not sure that AI has that sense. I don't think if you put in a thing for Claude and you say, "Tell me when we've gotten to the buying point," I don't think he's going to have a good answer. So when you ask about the role of AI in the investment process, I think that's an example of what it's not going to be able to do. Would you describe the way you're approaching markets right now as cautious? I'm always cautious until the time when I believe—because I'm inherently cautious and that caution has to be overcome. And by the way, a lender should be 80% caution. Being an uncautious aggressive investor is not a lender—is not a great idea. Because lending only has downside, it doesn't have any upside. The upside is that the contract is kept, the promises. But so I think we'll remain cautious until we believe that the disappointment and the declines have been profound enough to make it time to be aggressive. Then hopefully as we have in past cycles, hopefully we'll be the best most aggressive people on the planet and by the way maybe the only aggressive people left on the planet. We just have about a minute left. I'm curious what do you think people are underestimating today? Well, I think most people are underestimating the impact of AI. And you know, what was it—roughly 18 days ago on a Friday that a company called Block, which had 10,000 employees, announced that 4,000 of them were out? 40% of the workforce gone in one day because AI could do the work cheaper and faster. So you know, how many people in the world understand the potential import of that? That's what it is. Howard Marks, thank you so much for taking the time. Always wonderful to be you. Well, Lisa's always a great interview.

Inside the New Media Team with Marc Andreessen & Ben Horowitz

a16z(256.0K) ·46:31 ·5.5K views · 2026-03-18
Marc Andreessen (Co-Founder, a16z) Ben Horowitz (Co-Founder, a16z) Erik Torenberg (Host)
The modern media landscape has shifted from a defensive "corporate brand" model to an offensive "individual voice" model; success now requires "flooding the zone" with long-form, interesting content to outpace the 36-hour viral news cycle.
TL;DR The modern media landscape has shifted from a defensive "corporate brand" model to an offensive "individual voice" model; success now requires "flooding the zone" with long-form, interesting content to outpace the 36-hour viral news cycle. Traditional media relations...

TL;DR

The modern media landscape has shifted from a defensive "corporate brand" model to an offensive "individual voice" model; success now requires "flooding the zone" with long-form, interesting content to outpace the 36-hour viral news cycle.

The Strategic Shift: Offense Beats Defense

Traditional media relations focused on protecting a "synthetic" corporate brand and preventing leaks. In the new media landscape, the laws of physics have fundamentally changed.

  • Flooding the Zone: In the past, a negative article in a major publication was permanent and existential. Today, a16z counteracts negative press by appearing on dozens of podcasts to change the conversation and "erase" the memory of the slight through sheer volume.
  • The Power of Interest: Old media tried to please everyone and avoid offense. New media focuses exclusively on being interesting. If you are not interesting, you are invisible.
  • Zero-Cost Distribution: Because the "narrow media funnel" of TV and newspapers has been destroyed, creators can now bypass gatekeepers and speak directly to their audience at no cost, making offense a more viable strategy than defense.

The Death of the Corporate Brand

The era of the "vanilla" professional CEO is ending. Investors, talent, and customers now gravitate toward individual personalities rather than abstract corporate trademarks.

  • Human-Centric Communication: Marc Andreessen argues that the 80-year reign of "plastic" corporate branding was an unnatural consequence of limited media space. As channels have expanded, the public prefers to hear from the actual humans making decisions.
  • The "Joe Rogan CEO": Successful modern leaders (e.g., Elon Musk, Palmer Luckey) are "interesting enough to talk for three hours." This "Founder Go Direct" motion builds a level of trust and resonance that a sterile press release cannot replicate.
  • The Controversy Tax: To be powerful and interesting is to be controversial. Ben Horowitz notes that while corporate brands try to keep the "dust off," individuals must accept that being disliked by some is a prerequisite for being influential to others.

The Mechanics of the Viral Post

Using Marshall McLuhan’s "the medium is the message" framework, the speakers distinguish between the linear nature of TV and the spike-driven nature of the internet.

  • TV as a Morality Play: Everything on television is forced into a "TV show" format—a 20-to-40-minute story with clear heroes and villains and all the edges sanded off.
  • The 36-Hour Cycle: The native medium of the internet is the "viral post." These posts spike within 12 hours and usually disappear from collective memory within 36 hours as they are replaced by "the current thing."
  • Mainstream Media as a Follower: Major newspapers and TV stations no longer set the agenda; they spend their time "chasing the car" by reporting on viral posts that happened 24–48 hours prior.
  • Long-Form as a Shield: While short-form posts (X/Twitter) are prone to misinterpretation and "blowing up" a public figure, long-form content (podcasts, essays) provides the context necessary to protect complex ideas from being taken out of context.

Building the a16z Media Machine

a16z is moving away from traditional PR toward building a "network empire" that functions as a superpower for its portfolio companies.

  • Platform-Specific Expertise: Rather than cross-posting the same content, a16z hires experts obsessed with specific platforms (e.g., hiring an 18-year-old expert for Instagram).
  • Audience Targeting: Unlike old media’s "reach" (general audience), new media allows for "narrow targeting." a16z prioritizes reaching 90% of founders over 4% of the general world.
  • Launch as a Service: The firm has developed a systematic approach to company launches, achieving over 20 million views for 16 portfolio companies in 2024 through coordinated "new media" pushes.
  • Platform Roles:
      • X (Twitter): Remains the hub for "knowledge synthesis" and elite tech discourse.
      • TikTok/Instagram: Used for massive global reach and top-of-funnel awareness.
      • Podcasts/Substack: Used for nuance, context, and deep relationship building.

Notable Quotes

"In the past, there were maybe eight channels that could say something, and then whatever they said was permanent... Now, we could go on 30 podcasts... and erase that from everybody’s memory very quickly." — Ben Horowitz
"If it's on the internet, everything is a viral post... It’s a world where all the edges are sanded off and everything is kind of very smooth and professional on TV, but the internet is about things that make people flip out." — Marc Andreessen
"The job of a corporate CEO for a very long time was to get up on stage and to say absolutely nothing... and they would have been thrilled because they made no news. Mission accomplished. That world is just clearly fading." — Marc Andreessen
"Every single instinct you have is incorrect if you're in this world [of new media], and you just have to let it all go." — Ben Horowitz

An inside look at Pickaway County's Arsenal-1 | Interview with Anduril founder Palmer Luckey

WBNS 10TV(210.0K) ·33:18 ·36.9K views · 2026-03-18
Palmer Luckey (Founder, Anduril)
Anduril is launching "Arsenal-1" in Pickaway County, Ohio—a $1 billion, 5-million-square-foot facility designed to mass-produce autonomous fighter jets (the YFQ-44A Fury) at a "hyperscale" pace to restore U.S.
TL;DR Anduril is launching "Arsenal-1" in Pickaway County, Ohio—a $1 billion, 5-million-square-foot facility designed to mass-produce autonomous fighter jets (the YFQ-44A Fury) at a "hyperscale" pace to restore U.S. military deterrence. Founder Palmer Luckey aims to rebuild the...

TL;DR

Anduril is launching "Arsenal-1" in Pickaway County, Ohio—a $1 billion, 5-million-square-foot facility designed to mass-produce autonomous fighter jets (the YFQ-44A Fury) at a "hyperscale" pace to restore U.S. military deterrence. Founder Palmer Luckey aims to rebuild the "Arsenal of Democracy" by shifting defense from slow, taxpayer-funded contracting to a fast-paced, high-volume "product company" model.

Arsenal-1: Hyperscale Manufacturing in Ohio

  • Production Timeline: Anduril expects to begin production of the Fury autonomous fighter jet within weeks. The project moved from announcement to a near-complete second building in just one year.
  • Investment and Jobs: The $1 billion project is projected to create 4,000 jobs in Central Ohio over the next decade.
  • Facility Design: Unlike traditional defense plants, Arsenal-1 utilizes a modular layout without "monuments" (large, fixed concrete/steel structures).
      • Reconfigurability: The factory can be quickly pivoted to produce different systems, such as cruise missiles, depending on Department of Defense needs.
      • End-to-End Capability: The facility handles everything from wiring harnesses and sensor installation to stealth coating applications and radar signature testing.
  • Logistics: Completed aircraft will be flown directly from the neighboring Rickenbacker International Airport to customers.

A New Defense Business Model

  • Product vs. Contractor: Luckey emphasizes that Anduril is a "product company," not a traditional defense contractor.
      • Private Risk: The company invests hundreds of millions of its own capital into R&D and facilities rather than relying solely on government grants.
      • Incentives: Luckey argues that "living in fear" of losing private capital creates better results than "lounging on the taxpayer dime."
  • The "Twinkie" Concept: Luckey advocates for building autonomous systems that can be stored in warehouses for years ("like a Twinkie in a wrapper") and activated only when needed, avoiding the massive overhead of maintaining manned crews and ships during peacetime.
  • Economic Advantage: Autonomous weapons allow the U.S. to scale its fleet (e.g., from 300 to 1,000 ships) without the unsustainable cost of hiring and training tens of thousands of additional personnel.

Strategic Deterrence and Geopolitics

  • The Goal of Deterrence: The primary mission of Arsenal-1 is to make the cost of conflict so high that adversaries like China or North Korea never initiate a war.
  • Magazine Depth: Luckey highlights a critical vulnerability: the U.S. currently "burns through" years of weapon supplies in just days of active conflict. Arsenal-1 is designed to provide the volume needed for long-term endurance.
  • Global Competition: While acknowledging China’s superior manufacturing supply chain, Luckey believes the U.S. can catch up by leveraging its talent and capital, provided it cuts through procurement "red tape."

The Ethics and Accountability of AI

  • The Morality of "Smart" Weapons: Luckey argues that AI-driven weapons are more ethical than "dumb" weapons (like traditional landmines) because they can be programmed to distinguish between a military target and a civilian vehicle, reducing collateral damage.
  • Human Oversight: Luckey insists on civilian and elected leadership oversight.
      • Responsibility: Humans must remain accountable for the deployment and outcomes of autonomous systems.
      • Corporate Limits: Luckey explicitly rejects "corporatocracy," stating that tech executives should not have the power to unilaterally shut off military systems or dictate foreign policy.
  • Recruitment Philosophy: Anduril does not require college degrees for any role, including engineering. The company uses initiatives like the AI Grand Prix—a drone programming competition—as a primary recruiting tool to find "problem solvers."
"The goal here is not to build the weapons that will win a war with China. It's to build the things that will make China never want to go to war with us in the first place." — Palmer Luckey
"It's not a question of no weapons or smart weapons, it's a question of dumb weapons or smart weapons. And if I can build weapons that are able to reduce the number of civilian lives lost in war, I'm going to take that every single time." — Palmer Luckey
"I think that we do need to get comfortable with the idea that there will be certain systems that are making decisions of life and death and we need to be very careful about how we deploy them and how we take responsibility for the actions of those systems." — Palmer Luckey
Anduril this year will start producing autonomous fighter jets at its new Arsenal 1 production facility in Pickaway County. This is a one billion dollar project that will bring 4,000 jobs to Central Ohio over the next decade. The excitement is certainly building. And now on 10TV+, we hear from the man behind this vision: the founder of Anduril, Palmer Luckey. Thanks so much for joining us for this special presentation of 10TV+. I'm Angela An. Let's start with what we know today. Anduril first made its announcement of coming to Ohio in January 2025. Since then, it built out an existing spec warehouse to become the first Arsenal 1 building, and building number two here is near completion. When it is all said and done, the Arsenal 1 campus will encompass five million square feet. And that is what will be coming off the line by this summer in Pickaway County: the YFQ-44A Fury autonomous fighter jet. You're looking at a test flight last October after it took Anduril less than a year to build this prototype for the U.S. Air Force. Now I had the chance to talk exclusively with Palmer Luckey during his recent visit to Pickaway County. He met with local leaders, firefighters, even construction workers, saying he wanted to meet the people who are all part of history in helping to rebuild the Arsenal. Here now is my exclusive conversation with Palmer Luckey from inside Arsenal 1. Alright, Angela An here, exclusively inside the new Arsenal 1 facility with the one and only Palmer Luckey. How are you? Thank you for coming. Welcome to building one of Arsenal 1. Looking around, is this what you expected? Do you feel it? You know, it's pretty exciting. We've been expecting it for a while. There were a lot of people who said that we weren't going to be able to build a factory this fast. We announced the project a year ago. We've gone from announcement to this building and we actually just finished all the exterior building and construction on building two. So we're moving really fast. We're going to be starting fighter jet production here of the FQ-44 Fury in a matter of weeks. You say weeks. I mean, when you talk hyper scale, this is what hyper scale looks like. Fast, furious, we're on it. That's right. I mean, there's certain things that you cannot build at small scale at a reasonable price. There's certain things where it only makes sense if you can scale up production. That's true for a lot of cars, and that's true for a lot of consumer electronics projects. My last company was Oculus VR, which I sold to Facebook. And you know, you can't build one VR headset for an affordable price, but you can build a million of them. In the same way, you can't build one fighter jet for an affordable price, but you can build hundreds or thousands of them. You had the chance to meet with some of our local representatives, really the boots on the ground folks. I'm going to take a pause here because as we look around at this massive expansion, what is it that you wanted them to see first? So a lot of these are people who we've been working with for a year or more. And so the thing that I wanted them to see was that the things that we told them we were going to do, we actually came through. We've been hiring a bunch of people. We've been actually rotating those people out to our headquarters in California, training them, bringing them back here, and putting them to work on the line. We're actually building these facilities; it's actually working. We're getting all the permits we need and we're getting the customer traction we need. So I think most of my talks with local legislators and local leaders has been, "Hey look, what we said we were going to do, we actually did it." Because if I can get that track record, prove that I'm somebody who does what I say, and that I'm a man of my word, then they're going to trust me the next time that I come to them with something. They'll trust me when I say, "Hey, time to do even more. We're not going to end at five million square feet; we're going to start there." Walk me through. I see 22 stations around this huge massive facility. What is going to happen around us? So these stations have just been finished in terms of construction of the stations. We're about to start bringing in all the equipment for the work they've done. So different stations on different roles, you're going to have the aircraft moving down around this line as you have wiring harnesses installed, weapons systems installed, and sensors installed. You're going to have stealth coatings applied to the composite airframe and then, over there, tested to make sure that it actually has the low radar signature that the Air Force needs for this to be a useful platform alongside F-35s and F-22s. If you're going to have robot fighter jets, you need to make sure that they're coming off the line absolutely perfect. So this is everything you need to build a fighter jet from nothing all the way to a ready-to-go flying fighter jet. And we're going to be able to take those off right from Rickenbacker and fly those directly to the customer. What makes this so different than a traditional defense manufacturing facility? There are a lot of nuances here and there. I mean one of them is we've built this as a very modular facility. So if you look around you'll notice we don't have what we call monuments. By monuments, I mean big parts of the facility that are locked in place, trapped in place, made of concrete and steel, and welded together as an integral part. The reason for all of it remaining relatively mobile and easy to move, even things like our gantry cranes, is that you can reconfigure the factory. So if I need to scale up production or if I need to build a different aircraft, or suppose the Department of War comes to us and says, "We need you to focus on this particular model of the aircraft not this other one," or "We need you to make a bunch of this cruise missile in this part of the factory," we can reconfigure it to do that. So that's one of the things we've done differently. The biggest difference is Anduril is a defense product company. We're not a contractor; we're a product company. We use our own money to decide what to build, how to build it, and when it's done. We work with the customer closely in that process but we've invested hundreds of millions of dollars in this factory already because we believe in putting our own money towards these programs. I think that when we trip and fall we should lose our own money. It's good to live in fear, not just lounge on the taxpayer dime. And I think that makes a really big difference between Anduril and a lot of other competition. I'll end by saying this: the first page of our first pitch deck to our investors was that Anduril was going to save Americans hundreds of billions a year by making tens of billions of dollars a year. We're not there yet but we're getting there. Rebuilding the arsenal. We're trying to rebuild the arsenal of democracy. I love that clarification because I feel like, and you tell me if I'm wrong, that that might have been the most misunderstood part of what Anduril is doing with Arsenal 1. Sure. You mean in terms of what we're actually building here? Yeah, yeah. I mean look, what we're building here is not a weapons factory. What we're doing is we are trying to build the tools that are maximally deterrent to the enemies that the United States doesn't want to get in a fight with. The goal here is not to build the weapons that will win a war with China. It's to build the things that will make China never want to go to war with us in the first place. It's not to build the things that will make North Korea lose in a fight against South Korea. It's to make them realize that they can't win a fight with us. Now there are certain actors in the world who are irrational actors. You can't play game theory with people who believe that they win by dying. You're not going to deter them by building things like this at Arsenal 1. So there will always be fights with people that you can't reason with, but the things that we really need to be worried about as a country are toe-to-toe fights between superpowers and hyperspowers. And we are building the tools that we need to protect Americans, protect American interests, and protect American allies around the world by making it clear we are not a force that you can overcome. I have to segue; you talk about the conflicts around the world, obviously what's going on with Iran. Yep. You're watching very closely. Of course. We've... so I mean Anduril does a lot of work on U.S. bases all around the world including in that region, doing air defense, drone defense, and a variety of other missions. So we've been very deeply involved. Is Arsenal, or is Anduril I should say, and the building of Arsenal 1 going to give us that advantage that we need for conflicts like against Iran and other future conflicts? It's going to give us the advantage we need in the magazine depth that we need. One of the issues right now is that every day of this conflict, we're burning through months or even years of certain weapons systems. If our adversaries know that they can just wait us out or outproduce us, or that we'll fight a hard fight for 10 days and then run out, they're not going to be very threatened by that. They need to be of the belief that America can fight a war on day one, day two, day 10, day 100, day 1,000. That's what real deterrence is. Do you feel like autonomous weapons are what's going to win this weapons arms race? I do, for two big reasons. The first is that autonomous weapons can be used in ways that you could never morally use a manned aircraft. I can put them into harm's way. I can trade them against an enemy. I can put them out as the tip of the spear engaging in tactics that are likely to get it killed because the strategic outcome of that mission is worth it. Imagine trying to play chess without pawns. Imagine if you didn't have pieces that you could put in harm's way without fear. I think that autonomous weapons like Fury are going to be a critical part of the way that we deter wars and win wars. The other side is economic. The thing about autonomous weapons is that you can buy a very large number of them without having to increase the amount of manpower required to operate them. If I want to buy a ship, you know, I want the U.S. Navy to go from 300 ships to let's say 600 ships, I need to hire literally tens of thousands of additional sailors just to staff those ships and maintain them and build the parts. And then I have to sail them all over the world to keep everybody trained continuously. You kind of have to play pretend at being at war continuously when you have a manned system. With an unmanned system, I can build a ship, I can build a fighter jet, or I can build a submarine. I can put it in a warehouse like a Twinkie in a wrapper; it lasts for a thousand years. I was going to ask you about that. You compare weapons to Twinkies. I've talked about this concept before but we need to be building things exactly. And that's what allows us... now we can have a navy of not 600 ships but a thousand ships and we don't have to have the absolutely unmanageable cost of the people it would take to staff a thousand-person navy. When we can wait 10 years, 20 years, 50 years, we can break these things out and maybe you're going to put in a new brain chip or you're going to upgrade the sensors. I was going to ask; stuff is going to advance so much faster. Stuff is going to advance, but think about it this way: there are bombers over Iran right now that were designed almost 100 years ago. The United States is very good at taking systems that work and upgrading them and upgrading them and upgrading them. We're building Fury here in a matter of weeks. I think 50 years from today, there's going to be aircraft that look a lot like Fury and they're going to have new engines and they're going to have new sensors and they're going to have new weapons. But just like the F-15 or the F-16 or even the F-22, you're going to have these platforms flying for decades, maybe even a century. I've heard you talk about this when it comes to these autonomous systems. So my question is a little philosophical here for you: should AI ever be allowed to make that lethal decision about a human pressing a button? Reasonable people disagree but I strongly believe yes. And my reasoning is this: life and death, matters of life and death, are too important; they are too fraught to not apply the best possible technology to minimizing collateral damage and minimizing harm that need not happen. For example, people say things like, "Palmer, but can't you agree that an algorithm should never decide who lives and dies?" I say, "Well, I don't see it. I don't see the moral high ground in making, for example, an anti-vehicle landmine that can't tell the difference between a Russian tank and a school bus full of kids." And you could say, "Oh well, you know, we blew up the school bus but at least an algorithm didn't decide who dies." And I just... I can't see it because in the real world, the question that we have to ask ourselves is not a question of no weapons or smart weapons; it's a question of dumb weapons or smart weapons. And if I can build weapons that are able to reduce the number of civilian lives lost in war, I'm going to take that every single time, however spooky that might feel. Now the key is of course we need to hold humans accountable for that use of force. And that's where the question comes in. I think that the responsibility must lie with humans and we need people to take responsibility. Like if I'm deploying those landmines in a place where they're able to make a mistake and they make a mistake, a person needs to be held accountable. You need to say, "Did you use them appropriately? Did you trade the risks appropriately? Does this actually make sense?" It's the same thing that we use for existing air defense systems. If I turn an air defense system into full automated mode, shoot down anything that's incoming, and then it shoots down a guy in a Cessna who wasn't doing anything, there's going to be an investigation and they're going to say, "Did this warrant that?" "Were you in a situation that warranted this? Are you in an area that warrants this? Did you do enough of a review to understand what the consequences were?" The answer is not to say that you cannot have an autonomous air defense system. Because if you do that, all someone has to do is break communications or break your command and control or use an electromagnetic pulse that fries your command... that fries the laptop that controls it and now your enemy can pummel you without stopping. And so I think we do need to get comfortable with the idea that there will be certain systems that are making decisions of life and death and we need to be very careful about how we deploy them and how we take responsibility for the actions of those systems. And so if the systems were to say something happens, malfunctions, whatever, you would be the first to say, "Alright, we take responsibility, let's fix it, let's figure it out for the next step." Correct. And I'll go a step further. I think that people have actually been building autonomous weapons systems for thousands of years. They haven't been powered by AI exactly but you can go back 3,000 years and find records of the Chinese building pit traps. These were designed to let some soldiers walk over them and then the heavier soldiers carrying armor or horses fall straight through. That is a weapons system that is making targeting decisions based on the intent of the engineer who created it. Is it using AI? No. But the real question is, are we comfortable with technological weapons that act independent of the immediate oversight of a person? And the answer I think has to be yes with the right restrictions, with the right accountability. And the accountability by the way cannot be to corporate executives like me; it cannot be to executives who run AI companies. It needs to be to the people who elect civilian leadership that is tasked with overseeing the military and holding it accountable. Right, no, I get that. We can't live in a world where corporate executives have more practical power and control over U.S. foreign and U.S. military policy than the President of the United States, than the U.S. Congress. And there are people who I think would love to see that; I am not one of them. You don't want to be that; you don't want to be the Pentagon's "it" guy making those decisions. I want to be their "it" guy for making their weapons, for making their tools, but I need to be kept on a short leash, on a strong chain. I should not... you know, people come to me all the time, "Palmer, would you sell to this country? Would you allow your weapons to be used in this way or that way? Would you cut them off if they tried to do this?" I say, "This is crazy. You're asking me if I, as a corporate executive, should have the power to shut off parts of the U.S. military unilaterally against the will of the President of the United States or Congress or the people who elected them." Like, that is a dystopian future you should stay as far away from as possible. Even people who trust me, they shouldn't trust who comes after me; they shouldn't trust anybody else. These have to remain again in the realm of civilian elected leadership. People you can elect into power and that you can get out of power. Nobody's figured out a way to have the American people vote me out of my role, so I better not be in charge of these decisions. But could you sell to another country if you wanted to? So I can, with the cooperation of the United States government. What a lot of people don't know is I'm actually very constricted in who I can sell to. Based on what you're building for the Department of Defense. That's right. I mean if I were to try to sell to North Korea, I'd go to prison. And then at the same time, there's countries that the United States government wants us to work with. Sometimes for reasons that make a lot of sense publicly, sometimes for reasons people don't understand publicly. But you look back at for example the Cold War. There were times where people were really upset that the United States was allowing U.S. weapons companies to sell air defense systems to certain countries. They were saying, "Why are you selling to these autocratic regimes? Why are you doing this?" And then it comes out after the fact: it's because we secretly had nuclear weapons stationed on their airfields and it was critical that we not allow the Russians to come in and steal those weapons. The point is, the public is not always going to have every piece of information and sometimes they can't. But you have... if you believe in democracy, if you believe in the American experiment and if you believe that this constitutional republic of ours, flawed as it might be, is a better way to run a government than corporatocracy, then it's kind of the trade that you have to make. What worries you more Palmer? China's innovation or the red tape that sometimes we go through here in the U.S. to get things done? Well you know there's an old joke: if you want to sabotage China's war machine, just take the U.S. procurement manual, stamp "classified" on it and they'll steal it and copy it in no time. I think that it's both. I mean there's a lot of problems with the U.S. technology development and procurement systems. Certainly our manufacturing supply chain is not nearly as advanced as China. Look, I used to be a part of the problem. I made millions of virtual reality headsets in China at my previous job, Oculus VR. I started a virtual reality company when I was 19 years old, sold to Facebook, and worked on VR there for a few years before they fired me. I've spent a lot of time in China. I've had teams of hundreds of Chinese engineers. They know how to get stuff done. They are an incredible power. So if I had no national allegiance, I would be in Shenzhen, China for sure. It is the best place in the world to get stuff done very very fast if you are an engineer with a clear vision. Now there's advantages to the U.S. like there's certain things you can only build here; there's certain things where the talent is only here. There's certainly a lot of capital available here in a way that is not available in China. But like, we cannot take them lightly. They are very very good at making the things that we are bad at making. And until we're able to reverse that, we're going to be on the back foot. Do you feel like from what you heard with some of the leaders today, the local leaders, Arsenal 1 is turning that tide? You've promised 4,000 jobs in the next 10 years. You're partnering with our local schools. Engineering schools are back in business. Do you feel like you are building that arsenal of our workforce? I think so. And I think that that's going to be a mix of people that are doing traditional four-year college programs, people doing two-year degrees, even people doing things like six-month work training programs. Right out of the garage. Well look, I didn't graduate from college myself. I dropped out when I was 19 to start my first company. Anduril doesn't require a college degree for any job in the entire company. Any of them, even our engineering roles. And so the people who work here, there's going to be a mix of people who make different decisions about their life, about their career. We're going to have a place for all of those people as long as they want to work hard, work on problems that matter, and help us rebuild the arsenal. So you can see all these people looking at you right now. Like, do you feel like some responsibility? I mean you are making generational change for some of them. I feel a lot of responsibility and the thing that I remember every day is that Anduril... look, the people we hire, we're not their employer of last resort. Nobody is coming to work here because they have no other options, right? These are all smart people, motivated people. They could be starting their own company, they could be going to work at big tech companies, they could be going to other startups. The fact that they're here means that they support our mission and I need to in turn recognize that I can't take them for granted. These are people who have other options; I need to make this a great place to work, I need to make it a great way for them to support their families. I hope that when people eventually leave—because people probably aren't going to work for us you know until they keel over and die—but I hope that when people eventually leave Anduril, they look back and say, "Wow, that was professionally fulfilling, that was fiscally fulfilling, that was spiritually fulfilling." I want them to say it was the most fulfilling role I could have had in this time in my life. And I think that we're doing that. I want to talk about the AI Grand Prix real quickly because I mean that's so exciting. No one's done it and you said that's why I'm going to do it. That's right. So the AI Grand Prix is a competition that we just launched that is primarily targeted at people who want to be working on the latest cutting-edge autonomy technology. Most... it's a drone race but unlike most drone races which focus on how well you can be a joystick jockey—you know, how fast can I react, how well can I pilot it—it instead focuses on how well you can program the brain of a drone. So it's not about reaction time, it's not about how good you are with a joystick, it's about how good you are at typing on the computer and how good you are at coding. And that was something else that got me very interested in how you can use technology to help the men and women that are on the front lines of these fights who are coming back with real injuries, some obvious, some less obvious. And then I went into Oculus and building basically gaming and entertainment tools. I guess you could say Anduril is not so much a pivot into something new for me as a return to where I started. So I've loved the last eight or nine years that I've spent working on these problems. I think they're some of the most important problems out there. I think that there's not nearly enough people working on them. And if people don't work for me, I hope they work on these problems somewhere else because again, they're some of the most important problems we face and you can't wait until a war starts to start solving these problems. Because again, you want to build tools that stop the war from starting, not win it. And if you wait until the war starts to start working on these problems, you're just part of the killing, not the stopping. You think we can catch up? Because I feel like we got complacent a little while. So there's two things that you should keep in mind. One is that I'm very optimistic that we can catch up. In the areas where the American manufacturing side is behind, like supply chain... I believe we can catch up. But just as important as that is that there are people out there who think I'm wrong. There's people who think that we have to give up, that we should just give Taiwan to China, that we should just let China basically economically and politically dominate the world because it's too late. CHINA WARNS TRUMP: Trade probe risks major economic fallout

Fox Business(3.3M) ·21:24 ·168.6K views · 2026-03-18
Jamieson Greer (U.S. Trade Representative)
> "The Board of Trade...
TL;DR: The Trump administration is resetting the U.S.-China economic relationship through a proposed "Board of Trade" and broad Section 301 investigations into global excess capacity and forced labor, while reporting a 30% drop in the trade deficit with China over the past year....

TL;DR: The Trump administration is resetting the U.S.-China economic relationship through a proposed "Board of Trade" and broad Section 301 investigations into global excess capacity and forced labor, while reporting a 30% drop in the trade deficit with China over the past year.

U.S.-China Diplomatic and Economic Reset

  • Trip Postponement: President Trump has delayed his visit to China by approximately five weeks to focus on domestic issues and the conflict in Iran.
  • The "Board of Trade": U.S. Trade Representative (USTR) Jamieson Greer and Treasury Secretary Scott Bessent met with Chinese officials in Paris to discuss a new Board of Trade.
      • Goal: To establish stability and predictability by managing the trade of non-sensitive goods and addressing the trade balance.
      • Communication: Greer described recent talks as "constructive and cordial" despite the geopolitical tension surrounding China’s support for Iran.
  • Trade Deficit Trends: Greer reports the U.S. trade deficit with China has decreased by 30% over the last year, while the global deficit has dropped by 17% since April.

Section 301 Investigations and Tariff Outlook

  • Structural Excess Capacity Probe: The USTR is investigating unfair trading practices related to government subsidies and manufacturing surpluses that outstrip market demand.
      • Geographic Scope: Targets include East Asia, South Asia, Southeast Asia, Mexico, and the EU.
  • Forced Labor Investigation: A second probe covers 60 countries (representing 99.5% of U.S. trade) to ensure compliance with labor standards.
  • Timeline and Responsive Actions:
      • Public Hearings: Forced labor hearing scheduled for April 28; manufacturing/excess capacity hearing on May 5.
      • Potential Penalties: Outcomes could include new tariffs, service fees, or negotiated settlements. Greer expects "responsive actions" due to the rampant nature of global excess capacity.
  • Regulatory Tools: While Section 122 tariffs face legal challenges, Greer confirmed that Section 338 (addressing discrimination against U.S. goods) remains "on the table."

USMCA Renegotiations and Regional Strategy

  • Bilateral Approach: The administration is pursuing separate negotiations with Mexico and Canada rather than a trilateral approach.
      • Mexico Talks: Formal negotiations are currently underway regarding "rules of origin."
      • Anti-Transshipment: A primary goal is preventing Mexico from becoming a "hub" for Chinese or Vietnamese goods seeking duty-free entry into the U.S.
  • Canada Status: Greer noted that Canada is currently "behind" in the negotiation timeline compared to Mexico.

Domestic Economic Policy and Industrial Base

  • Manufacturing Shift: Greer highlighted a shift in import profiles toward capital goods (factory equipment and machinery), which has contributed to rising manufacturing productivity and a 5% year-over-year wage increase for factory workers.
  • Tariff Refund Controversy: Greer criticized Democratic Attorneys General for suing to strike down Section 122 tariffs, arguing that if refunds are legally required, the money should be diconnected from "multinational corporations" and given directly to workers as bonuses.
  • Defense Industrial Base: The administration is using tariffs to protect domestic production of drones, jet engines, and robotics, framing these as critical national security issues.

Institutional and Political Hurdles

  • Federal Reserve Deadlock: Greer expressed frustration over the "logjam" regarding Kevin Warsh’s confirmation as Fed Chairman, urging a quick resolution to ensure the Fed responds to current economic data.
  • Government Shutdown Impact: The USTR attributed a decline in recent GDP (stating it would have been over 3% otherwise) to the Department of Homeland Security shutdown, citing "economic fallout" from degraded border and travel operations.

One is on structural excess capacity, as you talked about, and one is about forced labor and making sure everyone has a law against importing goods made with forced labor. I think given how rampant the issue of structural excess capacity has been, and how long it takes to resolve this issue, you will almost certainly see some responsive actions if the investigation discovers what we suspect. What can you tell us about those negotiations? Well, right after this TV spot Maria, I'm going back to my office and I'm meeting with the Mexican Minister of Economy and his team. This week we are having our first formal negotiations with Mexico on the USMCA. And we are doing it just as the President said, we're having talks separately with Canada. We're going to be talking about rules of origin, which is a really a term of art that talks about how much how much content in the goods that are being traded between United States and Mexico are from United States and Mexico. We just don't want Mexico to become a hub for, you know, goods imported from Vietnam or China or somewhere else. If we're going to have some kind of a deal, it has to be a deal for Mexico and the United States and the goods should be made up of content for Mexico and the United States. So so less so on on the trade front. The reality is United States it it makes a lot of its own energy, it makes a lot of its own fertilizer, so there's less of a supply issue right now. Obviously you have things like nitrogen fertilizer, where the price does depend on the feedstocks coming out of the Gulf. That's something that I know Kevin Hassett and others are quite focused on. There are a variety of tools you can deploy to try to maximize production here, and and find additional sources and that kind of thing. You know, when it comes to a tariff or or trade, you know, you can't necessarily have a tariff modify and have that change the underlying fundamentals of the situation. And frankly with fertilizers we don't have, you know, we don't have and haven't had tariffs on fertilizers for for quite a while. But we're watching that very closely as a broader economic team, to make sure that the farmers can get what they need in time for their various plantings. Yeah, I know that Secretary Bessent has tried to increase imports of fertilizer and oil production via Venezuela, which certainly has had an impact. I want to switch gears and ask your take on on where we are with the Federal Reserve. I mean we got the Fed's decision obviously at PM Eastern and the President has said look, the the Fed should be cutting rates now and not waiting for this meeting. We're expecting the Fed to hold rate steady with Jay Powell speaking at at this afternoon. And this is his first speech since the President nominated Kevin Warsh as his successor. Unless Tom Tillis has changed his mind or something has happened that would cause that to happen, it is. Brian Armstrong - CEO of Coinbase | Podcast | In Good Company | Norges Bank Investment Management
