Again, very many of them outside of our country who are trying to come in. They're always going to be there. What you need your government to do, what you should expect your government to do, is to stay on top of it and keep you as safe as possible. That's what we try to do every single day. Thank you. Colin Jackson, Michigan Public Radio Network. We're seeing ICE expanding its presence in Southeast Michigan with developments near the airport on Romulus and office spaces in Southfield. And I'm wondering what you would say to community members who may be concerned that this might lead to an increase in aggressive immigration tactics like we've seen in Minnesota or Chicago? Well, what I'd say is that ICE has a very simple job. And it's not just ICE, it's the entire federal government, law enforcement agencies, the various, you know, three-letter agencies that exist within the federal government. It's to make people safe. And ICE in particular is trying to get violent criminals out of the United States of America. People who have no legal right to be in the United States of America, the best thing they could do is just go home voluntarily. That would be the easiest thing. And I always find it a little bit weird when congressional Democrats or others say, "How dare you?" and then the thing that they're so offended by is that we're getting illegal aliens, many of them with criminal records, out of our country. How dare we? That's exactly what we should be doing. That's what we promised we would do when the American people elected Donald J. Trump President of the United States, to protect the American people. You heard the last questioner, you know, asking justifiably about how we're keeping people safe. One of the ways that we keep people safe is by making sure bad people are outside of our country rather than inside of our country. If they're inside of our country, they should be inside of a jail cell, which is where they should be. The two exceptions, maybe three exceptions, are where? Minneapolis, Chicago and Los Angeles. Because in those three states, the local political leadership said, "Do not cooperate with getting violent criminals out of our state or out of our city." Now, I don't understand that. JUST IN: Jerome Powell Holds Briefing After Fed Holds Interest Rates, Warns Of Iran War Uncertainty

Forbes Breaking News(5.5M) ·48:16 ·2.8K views · 2026-03-18
Jerome Powell (Chair, Federal Reserve)
> "We are well-aware of the performance of inflation over the last few years...
TL;DR: The Federal Reserve elected to maintain interest rates at 3.5%–3.75%, balancing a resilient economy and stable labor market against "frustratingly" persistent inflation and new economic uncertainties stemming from the Middle East conflict. Status Quo Maintained: The FOMC...

TL;DR: The Federal Reserve elected to maintain interest rates at 3.5%–3.75%, balancing a resilient economy and stable labor market against "frustratingly" persistent inflation and new economic uncertainties stemming from the Middle East conflict.

Monetary Policy and Interest Rate Outlook

  • Status Quo Maintained: The FOMC kept the federal funds rate unchanged, viewing the current "modestly restrictive" stance as appropriate to guide inflation toward the 2% target.
  • Rate Cut Projections: The median Summary of Economic Projections (SEP) still suggests a policy rate of 3.4% by year-end, implying potential cuts, though Chair Powell noted a "meaningful movement" among several participants toward fewer cuts than previously anticipated.
  • Data Dependency: Powell emphasized that the Fed is not on a "preset course" and will make decisions on a meeting-by-meeting basis.
      • Policy Sensitivity: Rate cuts are conditional on seeing further progress on inflation; if progress stalls, rates will remain steady.
      • Two-Sided Risks: The committee is balancing the downside risk to the labor market (calling for lower rates) against the upside risk to inflation (calling for steady or higher rates).

Inflation Trends and the Impact of Tariffs

  • Persistent Elevation: Headline PCE inflation rose 2.8% over the last 12 months, with core PCE at 3.0%. Powell characterized the 1% overshoot of the target as a significant concern.
  • The Tariff Factor: A significant portion of current core inflation (estimated at 0.5% to 0.75%) is attributed to the "run-off" of one-time price increases from tariffs implemented last year.
      • Goods Inflation: Historically negative or zero, goods inflation is currently running at 2%.
      • One-Time vs. Ongoing: Powell views tariff impacts as one-time price level adjustments rather than a cycle of ongoing inflation, expecting this pressure to ease by mid-year.
  • Service Sector Stagnation: Non-housing services inflation has moved "sideways" for a year, which Powell described as frustrating given that the labor market is no longer a primary driver of inflationary pressure.

Labor Market and Economic Growth

  • Solid Economic Activity: Real GDP is projected to rise 2.4% this year. Consumer spending remains resilient, though the housing sector remains weak.
  • Labor Market Equilibrium: The unemployment rate has remained stable at 4.4%.
      • Job Creation: While official numbers show slowing gains, Powell noted that after adjusting for "over-counting," net private-sector job creation may be near zero.
      • Supply-Side Dynamics: Slower job growth is partially attributed to a decline in labor force growth following changes in immigration and participation.
  • Stagflation Rejection: Powell explicitly rejected the label of "stagflation," noting that current conditions (low unemployment and moderate inflation) bear no resemblance to the double-digit misery index of the 1970s.

Geopolitical Risks and Energy Shocks

  • Middle East Uncertainty: The conflict in the Middle East has already driven gas prices up by nearly one dollar per gallon.
  • The "Look Through" Policy: While central banks traditionally "look through" temporary energy shocks, Powell noted this approach is now complicated by five years of inflation staying above target.
      • Anchored Expectations: The Fed’s willingness to ignore energy price spikes depends heavily on long-term inflation expectations remaining anchored at 2%.
  • Economic Offsets: As a net energy exporter, the U.S. may see some economic offset through increased domestic oil production and profitability, though the net effect remains downward pressure on spending and upward pressure on inflation.

Long-Term Outlook and Institutional Stability

  • Productivity Gains: The Fed revised its long-run growth estimate up to 2.0%, driven by unexpectedly high productivity. Powell noted this trend began before the rise of generative AI, which may provide further boosts in the future.
  • Leadership Continuity: Addressing his expiring term, Powell confirmed he would serve as "Chair pro tem" if a successor is not confirmed by May 15th.
  • Investigation Transparency: Powell stated he has no intention of leaving the Board of Governors until the ongoing internal investigation is completed with "transparency and finality."

***

"We are well-aware of the performance of inflation over the last few years... the question of looking through [energy shocks] will be one to approach not lightly but in the context of five years of inflation above target." — Jerome Powell
"Effective zero net job creation in the private sector... looks like that’s about what the economy needs in terms of dealing with very, very low growth in the labor force. You've got a sort of zero employment growth equilibrium." — Jerome Powell
"I would reserve the term stagflation for a much more serious set of circumstances. That is not the situation we’re in. We have some tension between the goals and we’re trying to manage our way through it." — Jerome Powell
Good afternoon. My colleagues and I remain squarely focused on achieving our dual mandate goals of maximum employment and stable prices for the benefit of the American people. The U.S. economy has been expanding at a solid pace. While job gains have remained low, the unemployment rate has been little changed in recent months, and inflation remains somewhat elevated. Today, the FOMC decided to leave our policy rate unchanged. We see the current stance of monetary policy as appropriate to promote progress toward our maximum employment and 2 percent inflation goals. The implications of developments in the Middle East for the U.S. economy are uncertain. We will remain attentive to risks to both sides of our dual mandate. And I'll have more to say about monetary policy after briefly reviewing economic developments. Available indicators suggest that economic activity has been expanding at a solid pace. Consumer spending has been resilient, and business fixed investment has continued to expand. In contrast, activity in the housing sector has remained weak. In our Summary of Economic Projections, the median participant projects that real GDP will rise 2.4 percent this year and 2.3 percent next year, somewhat stronger than projected in December. In the labor market, the unemployment rate was 4.4 percent in February and has changed little since late last summer. Job gains have remained low. A good part of the slowing in the pace of job growth over the past year reflects a decline in the growth of the labor force due to lower immigration and labor force participation, though labor demand has clearly softened as well. Other indicators, including job openings, layoffs, hiring, and nominal wage growth, generally show little change in recent months. In our SEP, the median projection of the unemployment rate is 4.4 percent at the end of this year and edges down thereafter. Inflation has eased significantly from its highs in mid-2022 but remains somewhat elevated relative to our 2 percent longer-run goal. Estimates based on the Consumer Price Index and other data indicate that total PCE prices rose 2.8 percent over the 12 months ending in February. Excluding the volatile food and energy categories, core PCE prices rose 3.0 percent. These elevated readings largely reflect inflation in the goods sector, which has been boosted by the effects of tariffs. Near-term measures of inflation expectations have risen in recent weeks. This likely reflects the substantial rise in oil prices caused by the supply disruptions in the Middle East. Most measures of longer-term expectations remain consistent with our 2 percent inflation goal. The median projection in the SEP for total PCE inflation this year is 2.7 percent and 2.2 percent next year, a bit higher than projected in December. Our monetary policy actions are guided by our dual mandate to promote maximum employment and stable prices for the American people. At today's meeting, the committee decided to maintain the target range for the federal funds rate at 3-1/2 to 3-3/4 percent. From last September through December, we lowered our policy rate three-quarters of a percentage point, bringing it within a range of plausible estimates of neutral. This normalization of our policy stance should continue to help stabilize the labor market while allowing inflation to resume its downward trend toward 2 percent. But the implications of events in the Middle East for the U.S. economy are uncertain. In the near term, higher energy prices will push up overall inflation, but it is too soon to know the scope and duration of the potential effects on the economy. We will continue to monitor the risks to both sides of our mandate. We are well-positioned to determine the extent and timing of additional adjustments to our policy rate based on the incoming data, the evolving outlook, and the balance of risks. In our SEP, FOMC participants wrote down their individual assessments of an appropriate path of the federal funds rate under what each participant judges to be the most likely scenario for the economy. The median participant projects that the appropriate level of the federal funds rate will be 3.4 percent at the end of this year and 3.1 percent at the end of next year, unchanged from December. As is always the case, these individual forecasts are subject to uncertainty and they are not a committee plan or decision. Monetary policy is not on a preset course and we will make our decisions on a meeting-by-meeting basis. To conclude, the Fed has been assigned two goals for monetary policy: maximum employment and stable prices. We remain committed to supporting maximum employment, bringing inflation sustainably to our 2 percent goal, and keeping longer-term inflation expectations well anchored. Our success in delivering on these goals matters to all Americans. We at the Fed will continue to do our jobs with objectivity, integrity, and a deep commitment to serve the American people. And I would refer you to the statement that was in the Fed's brief that you will all have seen. And I won't have anything more for you on those issues. On the question of whether I will then continue to serve as a governor after my term ends and after the investigation is over, I have not made that decision yet, and I will make that decision based on what I think is best for the institution and for the people we serve. Figuring you probably were going to set the dominoes off there, Edward. And I'm not going to have any more to say on those issues by the way. Thank you very much. But actually that looks like that's about what the economy needs in terms of dealing with very, very low, non-existent really, growth in the labor force, which of course we've never had in our in our history. Do you think that is bad luck or is something changed in the world that makes supply shocks more common and does the central bank need to start taking into account of the risk of supply shocks as a more common problem? You know, we we did go through a long period where the shocks were all demand shocks. And, you know, so we've had a lot of practice thinking about supply shocks in the last four or five years for sure. And it's just a very different thing and a very much more difficult thing because it does immediately raise the question of tension between the two parts of our mandate. But, you know, has the world changed? I mean, COVID is a one-time thing, right? This energy supply shock is a one-time thing. It's not because of some broad tendency or anything I don't think. And and you know, the oil shock with Ukraine was also a consequence of military action. So I don't know that the world has changed in a way that there'll be more supply shocks, but I know there're people who have written that paper and that speech a number of times. There've been a number of people who have tried to make the case that that is the case. And in fact we have seen more supply shocks in the last five years than we've seen in many years before that. Add them together you get the misery index. And that's not the case right now. And what inflation is is ongoing increases in prices this year, next year, the year after. That's what inflation is. It's not a one-time price increase. There's a very, very big difference. The public doesn't really focus on that, but that's what the difference is. But the administration said they're going to get that rate right back up to where it was. So and we, you know, we assume they'll do that over time. So that's how we think about it. Thank you very much.

Vice President JD Vance Delivers Remarks in Auburn Hills, MI

The White House(3.0M) ·47:46 ·27.3K views · 2026-03-18
JD Vance (Vice President of the United States)
Vice President JD Vance and EPA Administrator Lee Zeldin highlighted the administration's "American Manufacturing Renaissance," centered on aggressive deregulation of the auto industry, tax relief for blue-collar workers, and a commitment to "America First" border and trade policies.
TL;DR Vice President JD Vance and EPA Administrator Lee Zeldin highlighted the administration's "American Manufacturing Renaissance," centered on aggressive deregulation of the auto industry, tax relief for blue-collar workers, and a commitment to "America First" border and...

TL;DR

Vice President JD Vance and EPA Administrator Lee Zeldin highlighted the administration's "American Manufacturing Renaissance," centered on aggressive deregulation of the auto industry, tax relief for blue-collar workers, and a commitment to "America First" border and trade policies.

Economic Policy and Manufacturing Growth

  • Manufacturing Renaissance: Vance highlighted the addition of over 2,000 manufacturing jobs in Michigan since the start of the administration, attributing growth to a shift away from offshoring.
      • Synergy and Robotics: Noted the interconnectedness of local firms like Engineering Design Services Inc. and FANUC, emphasizing Michigan’s rank as #2 in educational robotics adoption.
  • Tax Relief for Workers: The administration is promoting several key tax initiatives aimed at households earning under $100,000:
      • Specific Exemptions: Advocacy for no taxes on Social Security benefits and no taxes on overtime pay.
      • Take-Home Pay: Vance claimed that while the average worker lost $3,000 in purchasing power during the previous administration, they have gained $1,400 in take-home pay over the last 14 months.
  • Trade Fairness: Regarding trade with Canada and the Gordie Howe Bridge, Vance emphasized "basic fairness," stating that Canada must increase its own military spending and face reciprocal tariffs if they tax American goods.

Deregulation and the Auto Industry

  • EPA Overhaul: Administrator Lee Zeldin announced that the Trump EPA would finalize more deregulation in one year than previous administrations did in entire terms.
      • The "Endangerment Finding": Zeldin highlighted the removal of the 2009 Obama-era Endangerment Finding as the "largest act of deregulation in U.S. history."
  • Consumer Choice: The administration has moved to officially end electric vehicle (EV) mandates.
      • Regulatory Rollbacks: Eliminated "off-cycle credits" for start-stop features and reset CAFE standards to prioritize internal combustion engine production.
      • Market Impact: Vance cited a 2.4% rise in new U.S. vehicle sales in 2025—the largest jump since 2019.

Security and Immigration

  • Border and Crime: Vance linked the "wide-open border" to the fentanyl crisis and rising crime rates, arguing that safety is a prerequisite for the American Dream.
  • ICE Operations: Defended the expansion of ICE presence in Southeast Michigan (Romulus and Southfield).
      • Mission Focus: Stated ICE’s primary role is removing "violent criminals" and sex offenders who are in the country illegally.
      • Local Cooperation: Critiqued "sanctuary" leadership in cities like Chicago and Minneapolis, urging Michigan leaders to cooperate with federal enforcement to avoid civil strife.
  • Domestic Safety: Addressed the recent attack on Temple Israel, praising security guard Danny Phillips for his heroism and reaffirming a zero-tolerance policy for anti-Semitic violence.

Governance and Election Integrity

  • Administrative Cohesion: Addressing the resignation of Joe Kent over Iran policy, Vance stated that while the President welcomes diverse opinions, officials must "help implement the decisions" once made or resign.
  • Election Policy: Vance signaled strong support for the SAVE America Act, which would require voter identification for all federal elections to prevent fraudulent voting by non-citizens.
  • Energy Outlook: Characterized current gas price spikes as a "temporary blip" caused by Middle East instability, promising a return to "energy dominance" and lower prices within weeks.

— Lee Zeldin, EPA Administrator

Building great robotics here means American workers in Wisconsin could make things that they weren't making two years ago. The reason that has happened is because Donald J. Trump decided that we were sick of shipping American jobs overseas, we were sick of shipping American industry overseas—we were going to build right here in Michigan, right here at home, right here in the United States of America, and that's what we've done. Now, that said, as proud as we are of the great robotics that are happening right here and across the street, do you know that Michigan is the number two state in educational robotics adoption? You all should be very proud of this. I didn't know that until I was talking with somebody backstage. The number two state out of 50. And this is where the grief comes in: guess what's number one? Ohio. I won't even repeat it, because I'm going to get absolutely ran out of town if I say that. But we all know that as much as Michigan and Ohio have such a great rivalry, we have that rivalry because these are two of the states that are the proudest when it comes to building things and making things and creating the American Dream. And that's why I love this state. But I want to talk a little bit just about what the President of the United States and the entire team has done with the auto industry in the great state of Michigan. I know we're not in an auto plant, but this is an economy that depends on American automobile manufacturers, and it's one of the things that I'm proudest of that we did. We inherited a country where Joe Biden and the Democrats were trying to wage war against our American automakers. They were creating thousands and thousands of pages of new regulations, they were going to war against American energy, and they were trying to force every single person in this country to drive an electric vehicle. Then, about one year and two months ago, Donald J. Trump came into the White House and said, "You know what? We believe you ought to be able to drive whatever in the hell car you want in the United States of America because we want people to choose." In 2025, last year, new U.S. vehicle sales rose by 2.4 percent, which is the biggest jump in American-made automobiles since 2019, the last time Donald J. Trump was President. We've seen just in the last six months the effects of our tax policy start to make its way into the Michigan automobile industry. You know those working families' tax cuts that Lee helped get passed, and John James and so many of our great Michiganders helped make happen—that is real money in your pocket. For our businesses, that means they can hire more, they can create more jobs, and they can invest more in our great country. Those working families' tax cuts mean no taxes on Social Security. If you like no taxes on Social Security, remember: Republicans made it happen in Congress. If you like no taxes on overtime—because we've got a lot of great overtime workers just here in this place—you ought to thank a Republican in Congress because congressional Republicans made no taxes on overtime happen and Democrats fought us every single step of the way. And if you happen to believe that lowering taxes for people making less than $100,000 a year—and everybody else, by the way—is good policy, then again, we got to remember that it was congressional Republicans that made those victories happen. I happen to believe—I'm fundamentally an optimist about this country. I think that we have great Democrats, great Republicans, great independents—but the Democrats in Washington, DC are a different breed from the Democrats that exist in most of the rest of the country. You know, I was raised by two working-class Democrats; they were patriotic. My grandfather was a union steelworker for pretty much his entire life. I don't think he would understand the Democrat Party of Hakeem Jeffries and Chuck Schumer and Nancy Pelosi. They don't celebrate American victories, they don't vote for lower taxes for American workers, and they don't seem to be happy when America is doing well. Fundamentally, the reason I'm here is to get us to remember: we've got an election—it seems like it's very far off—but the reality is, in just about six months, you're going to be asked to go to the polls. You are going to be presented a question: Do you support the party that fights for fraud and illegal immigration, or do you support the Republican Party that fights for you, for lower taxes, and for good jobs right here in Michigan? I want to make sure I read off things that I learned about just on the airplane that are happening to this incredible state. You know that Detroit Diesel, one of the biggest engine manufacturers, announced it was adding a third shift and recalling laid-off workers that had been laid off last year. Why do you think that happened? That happens for two reasons: because we've got tax policies that promote the investment in the American worker, and because the President of the United States, for the first time in my entire lifetime, was willing to say to the rest of the world: you're done poaching our industry, you're done poaching our jobs. If you want to build right here in the United States of America, you get a big fat tax cut. But if you're trying to undercut the wages of American workers and bring in cheap garbage from overseas, you're going to get a big fat tax increase because we're protecting our workers and we're protecting the jobs that support them. So we eliminated the EV mandate. We eliminated a lot of the taxes that made it hard for us to invest in our communities. We had a President of the United States who was willing to go to war against those foreign companies and those foreign countries who were undercutting the wages of American workers. That is how you get the results that we have seen just over the last 14 months. I'll tell you—because I see him every day, and I talked to him this morning before I hit the road to come to Michigan—the President of the United States is proud of this record, but he is more impatient than any person I have ever met. One of the things I love about him is he will constantly tell Lee, me, and the entire economic team: "Let's celebrate our achievements, of course, but what have we done today? What have we done in the last week? What have we done in the last month to keep this progress going?" Now there's a statistic I'm going to toss out at you, but I think all of you have felt this statistic in your hearts. Under the Biden administration, the average American worker lost $3,000 in take-home pay. That was a combination of two things: higher prices—that terrible inflation problem that we had under Joe Biden's leadership—and higher taxes. Every single one of us got about an average of $3,000 poorer while Joe Biden and the Democrats ran Washington, DC. That is a very, very bad statistic. Now the good news is, over the last 14 months under Donald Trump's leadership, the average American has actually increased their take-home pay by about $1,400. Now that's a good thing—that $1,400 is money in your pocket. But what it also means is that a lot of our families are still struggling because of that terrible hole that Joe Biden and the Democrats put us in. For so many of us, the question in November is going to be very simply: Do we support the people who made us hundreds and hundreds of dollars richer, or do we support the people who made us hundreds and hundreds of dollars poorer? Let's support the people who made us richer, more prosperous, and more secure—and that's the congressional leadership in Washington, DC. I landed—I don't even remember which airport we landed in—but it was about a 20-minute drive from there to here. I was looking around and I was thinking to myself—again, this is why, even though I'm an Ohio State guy, I love the state—it reminded me so much of my hometown. You've got some of these beautiful old homes that I saw, places that hearken back to an era of American greatness when we built these beautiful buildings. Some of them looked amazing, and some of them, let's be honest, looked like they were struggling. I drove through some areas that were clearly struggling. And you know what I realized? That the American Dream was sold to the highest foreign bidder for pretty much my entire life. I'm 41 years old, and we said everybody—whether it was on Wall Street or a foreign company or a foreign competitor—was going to sell out the American Dream of the people right in this room so that somebody could make a quick buck. Or so that some foreign competitor could get rich off of the infrastructure that the United States of America had built over 250 proud years. What I realized driving through this area is that the American renaissance, ladies and gentlemen, being $1,200 richer in 14 months is a lot, but there is so much more that we can do together. There is so much more to be built, so much more to be reclaimed, and so much more to be made anew in the United States of America. It's not going to happen by going back to the failed policies of Joe Biden and Nancy Pelosi; it's going to be by doubling down on the policies of Donald J. Trump and the people who love this country. In the same way that the American Dream wasn't sold out in one year, it's going to take us longer to rebuild it. What is really at stake in this election in November is, fundamentally, we've done so much good and we've built so much. But the Democrats threaten to take us straight back to where we were just a few short years ago. We all know the economic statistics and the fact that we felt it in our pockets. Everything from groceries to our home electricity bill was going up at a level that it hadn't gone up in 40 years under Joe Biden. But it's not just that. Because the American Dream, my friends, as you all know, it's not just about going out and being able to buy something, as important as that is. It's not just about the economics, the dollars and cents, though of course we demand that our citizens become more prosperous. It's also about peace and security. It's about being safe in your own home. It's about what I think of as a classic American idea: that just because you grew up in a poor family like mine doesn't mean you shouldn't be able to walk down the street in safety and security in your own country. And that is what Joe Biden and his leadership, and Nancy Pelosi and all these clowns in Washington—that is the thing that they tried to take away the most from us. Where do you think the fentanyl that flooded our streets and killed hundreds of thousands of our citizens came from? It came from Joe Biden's wide-open border. Where do you think the highest crime rates and the highest murder rates came from during that administration? They came from the fact that we went to war on the police rather than on the criminals who committed crimes. Where do you think the fact that we had elevated murder rates not just in our big cities but in our small towns came from? We let in hundreds of thousands of violent criminals into the United States of America. So when I talk about the American Dream, I mean number one: that I want you to have a good job that pays you a good wage, that allows you to put food on the table but also take a couple of nice vacations every year. But what I also mean is that we need to get the people who are making our cities less safe the hell out of our country and focus on American citizens first. Because as much as we have achieved and as much as I'm proud of what we've done over the last 14 months, the Democrats threaten to take us right back to where we were the last time that they had control of the government. Think about the State of the Union address—it feels like forever ago, though I think it was only a month ago. As you all know, I have a very unique perspective on the State of the Union address. I'm the one person—the President's obviously talking, and I guess me and Mike Johnson, the Speaker of the House, are the two people who just get to see the Congress. We don't have anything to say—you stand up and sit down probably 150 times. It gave me a perspective on what's going on in our capital more than what's going on in our country. I realized that there is such cowardice in Washington, DC, in the political leadership in Washington, DC. I've never seen anything like it. You may remember this line—if you don't, I'll repeat it for you—but the President said something to the effect of: "If you believe that the government ought to put American citizens first rather than illegal aliens, stand up and cheer." Every Republican and like three or four Democrats stood up and cheered. What was funny is not just that the Democrats were sitting on their hands, it's that they were all looking around, like, "What do we do here?" What kind of a person is elected to serve in congressional leadership and has to look around at their peers rather than stand up and clap for the basic idea that the American government should be fighting for you, not people who are fraudulently in our country? What kind of a person can't stand up and clap for a six-year-old girl who was hugging her father because she was a little nervous being in the public spotlight? We were celebrating this six-year-old girl because she had survived a criminal attack from an illegal alien who shouldn't be in this country. And the Democrats couldn't stand and cheer at that. The thing that I took away from that is much more than any political issue or policy issue. I can stand up here and proudly say that we shut the border, we lowered your taxes, and we have the lowest murder rate in history. That is something I'm very proud of. But what I'm more proud of is that I am part of a political movement that is fighting for each and every single person in this room. That doesn't mean you're always going to agree with us. It doesn't mean you're going to agree with everything that congressional Republicans do in Washington, DC. What I do know is that they wake up and they think, "How can we best serve the people of this community and the people of the United States of America?" That is the biggest dividing line, and it breaks my heart to say it. The biggest dividing line in our politics is not tax policy or regulatory policy or even immigration, as much as you and I care about all these things. The biggest dividing line in our country is that we have in Washington, DC one political movement that is dedicated to fraud, to illegal immigration, and to making sure that this country is safe for them rather than safe for us. I would love to live in a country where when the President of the United States says, "It is the duty of the American government to look after American citizens first," for everybody, Democrat and Republican, to stand up and cheer. But my simple request is that until we get to that moment, until the Democrats can cheer for the idea that the government fights for you, until the Democrats can cheer for the fact that the government should be lowering your taxes, until the Democrats stand and cheer for the fact that the border ought to be closed and we ought to get the cartels out of our country—do not send those crazy people or any more of them to Washington, DC. Support the people who fight for you, and that is Donald J. Trump and the entire leadership of this party. Okay, so I've said everything, or at least most of what I want to say here, but I want to just give one note of shout-out to our fellow Americans in this community who have suffered a little bit in the last couple of weeks. Then I'll take some questions from the reporters. I believe we're only about a half-hour or so away from the facility, Temple Israel, the synagogue that was attacked by a terrorist just, well, not even a week ago, I believe. I think that all of us ought to keep these folks in our prayers, because when something happens to any member of our American family—in this particular incident, it happened to Jewish members of our American family—it is something that all of us have to stand up and say: it's disgusting, it's unacceptable, and we're not going to tolerate it in the United States of America. I hope all the folks from Temple Israel know that we're praying for them and we're thinking about them, from the President of the United States, the Vice President, and everyone else. We love you, and we're proud of how you've handled this particular situation, because it is tough. In particular, there was a police veteran who was a security guard by the name of Danny Phillips. Danny Phillips was one of the heroes that day. He was injured protecting the people in that community. I hope all of us will give a big round of applause and a prayer to Danny Phillips. He's the very best of this country, and sometimes you see the very best on our worst days. So with that, I will say thank you all for having me. The fun is not over yet. We'll take a few questions from the reporters. But if we could, what I'd like to do is start out with the local reporters first, and then if we have time, we'll get to the national reporters after that. Okay, go ahead, guys. Hi, Vice President. Brett Kast with WXYZ here in Detroit. Thank you for taking questions. We're seeing gas prices here climbing at home, in particular here in Metro Detroit, with what's going on with Iran and the impacts in the Strait of Hormuz. What is the administration doing to help keep gas prices down, and where do you see gas prices going here in the near future? Well, you know, the President of the United States has been crystal clear about this. Look, gas prices are up. And we know they're up, and we know that people are hurting because of it, and we're doing everything that we can to ensure that they stay lower. I will say—the President said this, and I certainly agree with it—this is a temporary blip, okay? What happened under the Biden administration is that gas prices were high for four years. Gas prices are higher right now, and frankly they're not even as high as they were during certain parts of the Biden administration because of what's going on in the Middle East. It's not going to last forever. We're going to take care of business, we're going to come back home, and when that happens, you're going to see energy prices come back down to reality. But in the meantime, we got a problem, we know that we have a problem, and we're doing everything that we can to address it. A couple of things, a couple of steps that we've taken just in the last couple of weeks, is that we actually worked with a lot of our allies all over the world who are suffering from this frankly more than we are. Unlike the United States, where we've pursued an energy dominance agenda, you've got a lot of people all over the world who have focused on a lot of green energy scams, and they're hurting a lot more than we are. So as much as we've got to focus on getting these gas prices down, the reality is overseas they're feeling it far worse than we did because we've taken the steps to protect our energy economy. For example, what we did working with these allies is we released hundreds of millions of barrels out of all these petroleum reserves so that we could put some downward pressure on prices. We're working on a number of things—in fact, a couple of things that I think will be announced in just the next 24 to 48 hours. I don't want to get ahead of the President on that, but we recognize this is an issue, we are fighting against it, and we promise that when this conflict draws to a close, we're going to see those energy prices come back down to reality. He delivered an energy dominance agenda, and it's made us much more secure in the face of these things. But yeah, we've got a rough road ahead of us for the next few weeks, but it's temporary. Next question. Hi. Remington Hernandez, News Channel 3 in Kalamazoo. The Gordie Howe Bridge is not too far from here. President Trump has made some comments about trade with Canada. For manufacturers here in Michigan, that bridge will be a lifeline to get products over the border. Do you think that the President has taken the right steps with Canada in regards to the bridge, and do you think that it should open on time? Well, the President's attitude to Canada is they are an important ally and they're an important trading partner, especially for folks in this region of our country. But Canada has taken advantage of the United States of America for far too long. They've taken advantage of the United States in a couple of different ways. Number one: they have allowed us to subsidize their entire military, their entire security. Canada pays virtually nothing, at least until a couple of years ago, for their own security, which means we had to foot the bill for that security. Is that fair to you and your kids, that you have to pay for the military of a foreign country? No. So the President of the United States said to the Canadians: number one, you guys got to take your own security more seriously. You're always going to be an ally, but why don't you have a little bit more self-sufficiency? And I think that makes complete and total sense. If we're going to be true friends, you have to treat our workers as fairly as we've treated Canadian workers for 40 years. Again, we have let so many people over the entire time that I've been alive take advantage of the United States of America. The President said: if you're going to apply a tariff to our goods, we're going to start applying a tariff to your goods too. It's basic fairness. And that's what we've done. Our hope with Canada—and I think we'll get there eventually—is we're going to get to a point where Canada treats our workers fairly, we treat their workers fairly, and they're going to be a true ally and a true friend, not somebody who's taken advantage of us, which is what they've done for most of my life. Thank you. Next question. Will Jones from Local 4. With the ongoing war with Iran and the recent attack that you just mentioned on a synagogue not too far from here, what is being done to keep Americans safe here at home? Yeah, a very good question. I believe that Tulsi Gabbard and John Ratcliffe are actually testifying before Congress right now to talk about some of these issues. Let me just say that it is an obsessive focus of the entire U.S. government to understand where the threats are, where people might attack innocent Americans, and what we can do to address it. We want to get ahead of it, and if God forbid something does happen, to respond to it as quickly as possible. There's a whole host of things that happen. Look, we're monitoring some classified stuff, some phone calls, things like that, so that we can try to figure out when foreign actors want to come into our country to commit acts of terrorism in the United States of America. You know, we've got the FBI searching for people who have come in, usually illegally, to the United States of America, who might wish the United States ill will. We're continuing to get illegal criminals out of our country because those are very often the people who commit acts of violence against the United States, against our people. So there's a lot that we're working on. Knock on wood, I think that we've had a very good track record with security under the Trump administration. We've got murder rates as low as they've ever been. But the only way to stay ahead of this stuff is to keep vigilant about it. The unfortunate reality is there are always bad people out there—again, very many of them outside of our country who are trying to come in. They're always going to be there. What you need your government to do, what you should expect your government to do, is to stay on top of it and keep you as safe as possible. That's what we try to do every single day. Thank you. Colin Jackson, Michigan Public Radio Network. We're seeing ICE expanding its presence in Southeast Michigan with developments near the airport in Romulus and office spaces in Southfield. I'm wondering what words you would have to say to community members who may be concerned that this might lead to an increase in aggressive immigration tactics like we've seen in Minnesota or Chicago? Well, what I'd say is that ICE has a very simple job. And it's not just ICE; it's the entire federal government, law enforcement agencies, the various three-letter agencies that exist within the federal government—it's to make people safe. ICE in particular is trying to get violent criminals out of the United States of America. People who have no legal right to be in the United States of America—the best thing they could do is just go home voluntarily. That would be the easiest thing. I always find it a little bit weird when congressional Democrats or others say, "How dare you?" and then the thing that they're so offended by is that we're getting illegal aliens—many of them with criminal records—out of our country. How dare we? That's exactly what we should be doing. That's what we promised we would do when the American people elected Donald J. Trump President of the United States: to protect the American people. You heard the last questioner asking justifiably about how we're keeping people safe, and one of the ways that we keep people safe is by making sure bad people are outside of our country rather than inside of our country. And if they're inside of our country, they should be inside of a jail cell. But on this issue of they're worried about aggressive enforcement: we have had ICE and immigration enforcement operating in 50 states, in red states and in blue states, in very, very blue cities and very, very red cities, without any incident. The two exceptions, maybe three exceptions are where? Minneapolis, Chicago, and Los Angeles. Because in those three states, the local political leadership said, "Do not cooperate with getting violent criminals out of our state, out of our city." Now, I don't understand that. If I'm living in suburban Minneapolis and I find out there's a sex offender illegal alien living three blocks away from me, I'm going to be calling the police and saying, "Get this person out of my country." But unfortunately you've had some Governors and a couple of Mayors who have said they're going to do everything possible to create civil strife by blocking immigration enforcement. What I would ask people in town to do, what I'd ask everybody in these communities to do, is just tell your local leadership that you want them to cooperate with getting bad people out of the United States of America. So long as we've got the cooperation and so long as we're working as a team, the chaos that you see in Minneapolis—it's not going to happen. It only happens when you've got broken and dysfunctional political leadership that tries to stop us from doing our jobs. Thank you for taking questions, Mr. Vice President. Craig Mauger from the Detroit News newspaper here. There's a lot of concern and talk in Michigan right now ahead of this pivotal midterm election that the federal government might somehow intervene in the administration of the election here in this state. Do you have any promises that you would make that the federal government will not intervene in any way in this election, or what would you tell people in Michigan who are concerned about that? Well, what do you mean by the federal government intervening in the election? That's the first I've ever heard of it, but maybe I don't understand your question. I believe the President has talked about nationalizing elections in recent months himself... Well, if what you mean by intervening in the election is that we want everybody to have a voter ID before voting in this country—yes, we should be doing that. To be clear. I mean, if by what you mean is, you know, Marco Rubio has like five jobs—is the President going to send Marco Rubio to Michigan to be a ballot counter as his sixth title? I don't think so. I haven't talked to the President or Marco about it. But look, there's a lot of scare tactics and a lot of scary language floating around there. The President and I have been working very hard, and we actually got a good vote yesterday in the United States Senate on the SAVE America Act, which is a very simple piece of legislation that does a number of things. But one of the things it does is actually establish this very simple principle that if you're going to vote in a federal election, you ought to show identification before you do so. I think that's very much common sense. So no, we don't want to intervene in elections, but we certainly want to make elections safe and secure. Number one: because we want to make sure that the will of the voters is actually recognized and respected. But think about this: we've got a lot of people in this room. Let's say for every single person here an illegal alien shows up and votes in a Michigan election. That isn't just fraudulent and that isn't just illegal; that steals your right to vote away from you, because then your vote counts for less. If you believe as I do that the American people are sovereign in their own country, then you have to believe that their vote should be protected, and the only way to fully protect their vote is to prevent fraudsters from coming and voting in American elections. And we'll take just one more question. Hello, Mr. Vice President. Ben Solis, Michigan Advance. Joe Kent resigned from his post over his objection to the war in Iran. He was among those Republicans who was pretty dedicated to Trump's agenda and the MAGA movement. So how does his exit speak to the fracture of that coalition over the war, and what can you say to those who deeply believe in the President's agenda but are really worried about the consequences of what the war in Iran holds? Well, look, nobody likes war, right? And I guarantee the President of the United States is not interested in getting us in the kind of long-term quagmires that we've seen in years past. I know the President, and I know the way that he thinks about America's national security—that is not a risk with this President at all. What he has also said consistently for 10, 15 years, maybe even longer, is Iran cannot have a nuclear weapon, and he's willing to take action—diplomatic, ideally, but military action if he has to—to make sure that that doesn't happen. That's all this is about. We don't want the Iranians to have a nuclear weapon. The President's been clear about this, and that's what led to the President's decision of about three weeks ago. Now you ask about Joe Kent. Now I know Joe Kent a little bit. I like Joe Kent. You heard the President of the United States say yesterday that he likes Joe Kent too. But it's one thing to have a disagreement of opinion. I know the President very well: he welcomes differences of opinion. He likes it when people express their views about what should happen—he listens to everybody. It's one of the great things I like about him: that whether you're the gardener at Mar-a-Lago or whether you're the Secretary of State, the President cares about what you think about an issue. He recognizes that everybody has smarts and everybody has wisdom. That said, whatever your view, when the President of the United States makes a decision, it's your job to help make that decision as effective and successful as possible. So the President said this yesterday: if you are on the team and you can't help implement the decisions of his administration—he has the right to make those decisions—then it's a good thing for you to resign. I think that's exactly right. It's fine to disagree. But once the President makes a decision, it's up to everybody who serves in his administration to make it as successful as possible. That's how I do my job, and I think that's how everybody in the administration should do their job too. So let me just say this on a closing note here. You know, we're here in the great state of Michigan. I'll head back to DC this evening. I just want to say that I know that you guys are the people who actually make this country run. A very formative moment for me, just for a lot of reasons and in part because all of our leaders went completely insane, was the COVID pandemic. Remember how they shut everything down? I kept thinking to myself that I know a lot of people who worked in manufacturing, and I know a lot of people who continued to go to work because they didn't have any other choice. Frankly, while the United States of America would do just fine with a few fewer people on Wall Street on Microsoft Excel, America doesn't do fine at all if our manufacturers and the people who build and make things don't show up for work and make sure that America has the stuff that it needs. One of the ways that we think about our administration, and one of the ways that we think about our goal, is that we serve you. You fought for us for long enough. We have to fight for you. You've done your job for the United States of America and you're going to keep on doing it. It's time to have a government that works for you. I mentioned this earlier: for my entire life, the story of my hometown was another factory closed, another place that provided good jobs to the moms and dads of my friends was going overseas, and another family that faced financial stress because mom or dad lost a job was getting divorced because of the financial pressures created by stupid policies coming out of Washington, DC. I just want you to know—I said this the first time I ever spoke as the Vice Presidential nominee, and I'll repeat it right now—I will never forget where I came from. Part of what we're trying to do, the biggest part of what we're trying to do, is stop the bleeding and reverse it. Where factories were closing down for my entire life, now factories are being built back up. Where engine manufacturers were shutting down shifts, now they're adding shifts. Where people are hiring instead of firing our great American workers. That is what we're trying to do in the Trump administration. 14 months in, I think that we've got a lot to be proud of and a lot to build on and a lot to keep working at. But I just want you to know—no matter what you see come out of Washington from the crazy radicals, and no matter what the media reports on and tells you, the President of the United States sends this message and the Vice President of the United States agrees with it wholeheartedly. You are the beating heart of the United States of America and the Trump administration will never stop fighting for you every single day. God bless you all, and thank you for having me. Thank you guys, I appreciate it. Thank you guys. Bye, bye.

Federal Reserve Chair Jerome Powell speaks after Fed holds interest rate steady — 3/18/2026

CNBC Television(3.3M) ·48:36 ·37.5K views · 2026-03-18
Jerome Powell (Chair, Federal Reserve)
The Federal Reserve held interest rates steady at 3.5%–3.75% as it navigates a "difficult situation" characterized by upside risks to inflation from Middle East oil shocks and tariffs, contrasted against downside risks in a cooling labor market.
TL;DR The Federal Reserve held interest rates steady at 3.5%–3.75% as it navigates a "difficult situation" characterized by upside risks to inflation from Middle East oil shocks and tariffs, contrasted against downside risks in a cooling labor market. Rate Decision: The FOMC...

TL;DR

The Federal Reserve held interest rates steady at 3.5%–3.75% as it navigates a "difficult situation" characterized by upside risks to inflation from Middle East oil shocks and tariffs, contrasted against downside risks in a cooling labor market.

Monetary Policy and Interest Rates

  • Rate Decision: The FOMC voted 11-1 to maintain the benchmark federal funds rate at 3.5%–3.75%, following 75 basis points of cuts between September and December.
  • Policy Stance: Chair Powell characterized the current rate as being on the "higher borderline" of neutral to "modestly restrictive."
  • Future Projections (SEP):
      • The median participant projects the federal funds rate at 3.4% by the end of 2026 and 3.1% by the end of 2027.
      • While the median remained unchanged from December, Powell noted a "meaningful" shift toward fewer projected cuts, with several officials moving from two projected cuts to one.
  • Decision Framework: Policy is not on a preset course; the Fed remains in a "wait-and-see" mode, balancing the risk of cutting too early (stoking inflation) versus cutting too late (weakening the labor market).

Inflation and the Impact of Shocks

  • Current Readings: Total PCE inflation rose 2.8% over the last 12 months, while core PCE (excluding food and energy) stands at 3.0%.
  • Tariff Pressures: Powell attributed a significant portion of core inflation—between one-half and three-quarters—to the ongoing effects of tariffs on the goods sector. He noted that goods inflation is currently running at 2% compared to its historical norm of zero or negative.
  • Energy and Geopolitics: The conflict in the Middle East has driven a "substantial rise" in oil prices (up nearly $1.00 per gallon at the pump).
      • "Looking Through" Shocks: While the Fed traditionally "looks through" temporary energy shocks, Powell noted this is harder to do after five years of inflation remaining above the 2% target.
  • Service Sector Persistence: Non-housing services have moved "sideways" for a year, failing to show the disinflationary progress seen in other sectors despite a cooling labor market.

Labor Market and Economic Growth

  • Stagnant Job Growth: While the unemployment rate is stable at 4.4%, Powell revealed that after adjusting for "overcounting," there is essentially zero net job creation in the private sector.
  • Labor Supply: Slowing job growth is partly due to lower immigration and labor force participation, leading to what Powell called a "zero employment growth equilibrium."
  • GDP and Productivity: Real GDP is projected to rise 2.4% this year. Powell highlighted surprisingly "high productivity" over the last four to five years as a primary driver of growth, though he noted that the infrastructure demands of Generative AI (e.g., building data centers) may actually be pushing inflation up in the near term.
  • Stagflation Concerns: Powell dismissed the term "stagflation," noting that while there is tension between mandate goals, current conditions do not resemble the double-digit unemployment and high inflation of the 1970s.

Institutional Stability and Leadership

  • Succession Plan: Powell’s term as Chair is nearing its end. He confirmed that if a successor is not confirmed by May 15, he will serve as "Chair pro tem" to ensure institutional continuity.
  • Ongoing Investigation: Powell stated he has no intention of leaving the Board of Governors until an ongoing investigation is completed with "transparency and finality." He has not yet decided if he will serve out his remaining term as a Governor after the investigation concludes.

Notable Quotes

"If you adjust what has been the trend job creation over the past let’s say six months... effectively there’s zero net job creation in the private sector. ... It does have a feel of downside risk." — Jerome Powell
"We’re well aware of the performance of inflation over the last few years... and now there will be some effects on inflation coming forward. The question of looking through [energy shocks]... will be one to approach not lightly." — Jerome Powell
"We are balancing these two goals in a situation where the risks to the labor market are to the downside... and the risks to inflation are to the upside. We’re in a difficult situation." — Jerome Powell
"I have no intention of leaving the board until the investigation is well and truly over, with transparency and finality." — Jerome Powell
Progress toward our maximum employment and 2% inflation goals. The implications of developments in the Middle East for the U.S. economy are uncertain. We will remain attentive to risks to both sides of our dual mandate, and I'll have more to say about monetary policy after briefly reviewing economic developments. Available indicators suggest that economic activity has been expanding at a solid pace. Consumer spending has been resilient, and business fixed investment has continued to expand. In contrast, activity in the housing sector has remained weak. In our summary of economic projections, the median participant projects that real GDP will rise 2.4% this year and 2.3% next year, somewhat stronger than projected in December. In the labor market, the unemployment rate was 4.4% in February and has changed little since late last summer. Job gains have remained low. A good part of the slowing in the pace of job growth over the past year reflects a decline in the growth of the labor force, due to lower immigration and labor force participation, though labor demand has clearly softened as well. Other indicators, including job openings, layoffs, hiring, and nominal wage growth, generally show little change in recent months. In our SEP, the median projection of the unemployment rate is 4.4% at the end of this year and edges down thereafter. Inflation has eased significantly from its highs in mid-2022 but remains somewhat elevated relative to our 2% longer-run goal. Estimates based on the Consumer Price Index and other data indicate that total PCE prices rose 2.8% over the 12 months ending in February and that, excluding the volatile food and energy categories, core PCE prices rose 3.0%. These elevated readings largely reflect inflation in the goods sector, which has been boosted by the effects of tariffs. Near-term measures of inflation expectations have risen in recent weeks, likely reflecting the substantial rise in oil prices caused by the supply disruptions in the Middle East. Most measures of longer-term expectations remain consistent with our 2% inflation goal. The median projection in the SEP for total PCE inflation this year is 2.7% and 2.2% next year, a bit higher than projected in December. Our monetary policy actions are guided by our dual mandate to promote maximum employment and stable prices for the American people. At today's meeting, the committee decided to maintain the target range for the federal funds rate at 3.5 to 3.75 percent. From last September through December, we lowered our policy rate three-quarters of a percentage point, bringing it within a range of plausible estimates of neutral. This normalization of our policy stance should continue to help stabilize the labor market while allowing inflation to resume its downward trend toward 2%. But the implications of events in the Middle East for the U.S. economy are uncertain. In the near term, higher energy prices will push up overall inflation, but it is too soon to know the scope and duration of the potential effects on the economy. We will continue to monitor the risks to both sides of our mandate. We are well-positioned to determine the extent and timing of additional adjustments to our policy rate based on the incoming data, the evolving outlook, and the balance of risks. In our SEP, FOMC participants wrote down their individual assessments of an appropriate path of the federal funds rate under what each participant judges to be the most likely scenario for the economy. The median participant projects that the appropriate level of the federal funds rate will be 3.4% at the end of this year and 3.1% at the end of next year, unchanged from December. As is always the case, these individual forecasts are subject to uncertainty, and they are not a committee plan or decision. Monetary policy is not on a preset course, and we will make our decisions on a meeting-by-meeting basis. To conclude, the Fed has been assigned two goals for monetary policy: maximum employment and stable prices. We remain committed to supporting maximum employment, bringing inflation sustainably to our 2% goal, and keeping longer-term inflation expectations well-anchored. Our success in delivering on these goals matters to all Americans. We at the Fed will continue to do our jobs with objectivity, integrity, and a deep commitment to serve the American people. And you know, I think it's important that we do that, very important. Nick Timiraos, the Wall Street Journal. Chair Powell, you've described monetary policy as modestly restrictive. So goods inflation is running at 2%, so that's not coming from standard Phillips curve restrictive policy; it's coming from the run-off of a one-time thing. We also think it's important though to keep policy either mildly restrictive or close to that, but not too restrictive because of the weakness in the downside risk in the labor market. We are balancing these two goals in a situation where the risks to the labor market are to the downside, which would call for lower rates, and the risks to inflation are to the upside, which would call for higher rates or not cutting anyway. So we're in a difficult situation and we feel like our framework calls on us to balance the risks, and we feel like where we are now is just kind of on that borderline, the higher borderline of restrictive versus not restrictive. The upward risk for inflation, the downward risk for employment, and that puts us in a different situation. You know, when we use the term stagflation, I always have to point out that that was a 1970s term at a time when unemployment was in double figures and inflation was really high and the misery index was super high—add them together you get the misery index. And that's not the case right now. We actually have unemployment really close to longer-run normal, and we have inflation that's, you know, 1 percentage point above that. So calling that stagflation, it's not—you know, I would reserve the term stagflation for, you know, a much more serious set of circumstances. That is not the situation we're in. What we have is some tension between the goals and we're trying to manage our way through it. It's a very difficult situation but it's nothing like what they faced in the 1970s in that I reserve stagflation for that—the word for that period. Maybe that's just me. And you know, we're going to see whether the—what happens is going to be very important for the way the economy looks and the way the outlook evolves with what happens in the Middle East. That's going to be a big factor and we'll know that then. I don't know how it's going to affect our thinking, I really don't. And we did talk about alternative scenarios a little bit but it's just I wouldn't bring that in here. It's very uncertain and I just want to just remember that we don't know and we shouldn't assume it's going to be one thing or another. We're going to see. So is it a matter of you don't know how the situation itself is going to evolve, or even if things remain as they are, the economy could still remain relatively durable in face of them? Yeah, that's true too. No, I mean, the U.S. economy has just made—it's been strong through a whole bunch of challenges. I mean if you go back to 2023 when we raised rates a lot in '22 and '23 and close to 100% of economists called for a recession which didn't happen. In fact, 2023 was a really strong year. So I—the U.S. economy has really been just doing pretty well through a lot of significant challenges over the past few years. It's been amazing to see and again, I don't—I don't know what's going to happen in the next intermediate cycle and I don't know what's going to happen in the Middle East, I wouldn't want to speculate. Just to sort of stare into the future for a little bit, I was looking at the longer-run bits of the SEP and noticed that the growth estimate had been revised up a good chunk from 1.8 to 2 and the kind of long-term fed funds rate as well. It'd be great just to hear—is that AI productivity or what's going on there exactly? Yeah, well I think it's just productivity. You know, we actually started seeing meaningfully higher productivity some years ago, four years ago five years ago, and and that's not because of, you know, generative AI. It may—we'll never—we won't know for years what it's really due to, but it could be due to the kind of things that people did during the pandemic to economize and somehow become more productive because there was a, you know, an incredible labor shortage. But and I—you know, I think economic forecasters are very skeptical of periods of high productivity because they're so rare and they're often revised away. And for many of us, I never thought I'd see this many years of really high productivity and by the way expect it to continue. And we haven't really started to see the effects of generative AI and that should—that should certainly contribute to that. So it's quite unusual and it's—you know, this higher productivity is the thing that allows incomes to rise over time and so it's a great thing. Was that your question? Yeah, no, I was just curious what lay behind that. And just briefly on that as well to finish up. You hear the argument a lot this is a sort of disinflationary story and if you believe that productivity thing that should also weigh down on inflation over time, maybe then kind of pull down where rates end up. Do you kind of agree with that line of thinking or would you think that's not how you should think about where rates end up? I think you have to be cautious about that, in particular if you're talking about AI, about generative AI. So remember in the short term, what's happening is we're building data centers everywhere. And that's actually putting pressure on all kinds of goods and services that go into building these things. So that's actually probably pushing inflation up at the margin. In addition, it probably raises the neutral rate. So in the near term, you're looking for—you're not looking for something that would immediately call for lower rates or that would be lowering inflation. Over time, though, sure, it can be a—if it's expanding potential output, which is what productivity, higher productivity does, then it actually can be. But I think it's an empirical question because it's really—is the demand growing faster or slower than the supply side? And I think we just don't know that answer ex ante going in. We'll just have to wait and see. Thank you very much.

Balaji Srinivasan: Investing in the Age of American Anarchy | The Bitcoin Magazine Podcast

Bitcoin Magazine(255.0K) ·74:52 ·1.7K views · 2026-03-18
Balaji Srinivasan (Investor and Author)

TL;DR

Balaji Srinivasan argues that as the American political system oscillates toward collapse (“American Anarchy”), the future lies in “Network States”—decentralized, cloud-first societies built on shared values, Bitcoin, and AI-driven productivity. He advises individuals to prioritize physical mobility and digital sovereignty through a strategy of “liquidate, emigrate, and accelerate.”

The Network State: Rebundling Society

  • Cloud-First, Land-Last: The Network State is a new way to start a society by building a cloud community first, which then crowdfunds physical land (decentralized "data centers" for people) across the globe.
      • Statism for Anarchists: It is not "pure libertarianism" (like refusing seatbelts); it seeks to build functional, legitimate hierarchies similar to Singapore—low crime, high order, and reasonable regulation.
  • Network Neighbors: The internet has unbundled people from physical locations; the next step is "rebundling" them into startup societies where digital neighbors with shared values eventually become physical neighbors.
  • The Network School: Srinivasan’s "Network School" (ns.com) acts as a meta-startup society, bootstrapping other communities focused on specific innovations like biotech (Prospera) or walkability (Culdesac).

Bitcoin as the Civilizational “Fire Alarm”

  • Conquering Minds: Bitcoin’s growth is measured by the "territory of minds" it occupies. It has expanded from Satoshi to every ethnic and religious group globally.
  • The Judiciary Layer: In the trifecta of government (Executive, Legislative, Judiciary), Bitcoin automates the judiciary. Code determines the finality of transactions, removing fallible human judgment from the monetary system.
  • Disciplining the Fed: Bitcoin acts as a "fire alarm"—the higher the price, the more it signals that the legacy economy is breaking. It forces central banks to "reform via exit" rather than through political "voice."
  • Return of Earn.com: Srinivasan revealed plans to relaunch the "earn Bitcoin for tasks" model via ns.com to combat the upcoming surge of AI-generated spam and scams.

AI and the Rise of Tribal Productivity

  • Trusted Tribes: AI boosts productivity significantly *within* a trusted tribe (e.g., sharing a code repository). However, *outside* the tribe, AI produces "spam, scams, and slop."
  • Web3 of Trust: To survive the AI era, communication must be gated by cryptographic signatures. Users will likely stop receiving messages from anyone outside a certain degree of their "Web3 of trust."
  • The Global Dyad: The future is a "billion-person Chinese superstate" vs. "a thousand million-person network states."
      • China: Represents "Harmony" and a physical-world lead in robotics/manufacturing.
      • The Internet: Represents "Freedom" and a lead in digital-world innovation and decentralization.

The Theory of “American Anarchy”

  • Galloping Gertie: Srinivasan compares the US to the Tacoma Narrows Bridge, which swung further left and right until it collapsed. He believes the US is not in a "pendulum swing" but a "breakage" phase.
  • The Anarchy Trap: Unlike the East (which fears chaos and values security/harmony), the West is hypersensitive to hierarchy. This "Left-Libertarianism" and "Right-Libertarianism" combined lead to tribal warlordism rather than a functional state.
  • Civilizational Antibodies: Countries like El Salvador (hard money/anti-crime) and India (new infrastructure/Apple stores) have developed "antibodies" against the money printing and socialism that are currently rotting the US from within.

Executive Action: Liquidate, Emigrate, Accelerate

  • Location > Allocation: In a crisis, a peaceful location is more valuable than a high-net-worth allocation. Srinivasan advises "liquidating" physical US assets (like houses at the top of a bubble) and "emigrating" to jurisdictions with high-trust leaders.
  • Target Destinations: He identifies "Elon Salvador" (El Salvador), Texas (Starbase), Miami, and Bangalore as high-energy hubs.
  • The Pseudonymous Economy: He recommends the next generation use AI voice/face changers and high-quality pseudonyms to detach their real identities from their digital presence to avoid government "grabby-ness" and wealth taxes.
  • The "Elon Salvador" Vision: Srinivasan suggests a "magic wand" solution: Bukele gives Elon Musk a special economic zone to build "humanoid robots and Neuralinks" at the speed of physics rather than permits.
“Bitcoin is a fire alarm that tells you something is wrong. The higher Bitcoin goes, the wronger things are in the world economy. It’s clanging all the way up.” — Balaji Srinivasan
“Liquidate, emigrate, accelerate. A second passport is a much better investment than a first home right now.” — Balaji Srinivasan
“In the West, it’s so decentralized and disordered that you actually have not totalitarianism, but anarchy, as where America is going.” — Balaji Srinivasan
“The person 40 feet above you in an apartment building is a stranger, but your digital neighbor 3,000 miles away shares your values and currency. Network states make those digital neighbors your physical neighbors.” — Balaji Srinivasan
